                                                          BEYOND ENDOSCOPY FOR GL(3, Q): POISSON SUMMATION

                                                                                 TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA




arXiv:2603.21506v1 [math.NT] 23 Mar 2026
                                                   Abstract. We generalize to GL(3, Q) the Poisson Summation method developed by Altuğ for GL(2, Q) for
                                                   the strategy of Beyond Endoscopy. Concretely, assuming Conjecture A, we isolate the contribution of the
                                                   trivial representation from the regular elliptic part of the trace formula and obtain a concrete expansion of
                                                                                                 Iell (f ) − Tr(1(f )).
                                                   Our starting point is a reformulation of the regular elliptic part in terms of cubic orders attached to charac-
                                                   teristic polynomials. To these orders we associate a zeta function, defined through their overorders, prove a
                                                   functional equation for its completion, and apply an approximate functional equation to rewrite the elliptic
                                                   term in a form suitable for Poisson Summation. A key arithmetic input is a periodicity theorem showing
                                                   that the relevant coefficients depend only on the parameters modulo a finite modulus. This makes it possible
                                                   to perform Poisson Summation on the integral parameters indexed by the cubic data.
                                                      The resulting main terms are governed by Kloosterman-type sums and an associated double Dirichlet
                                                   series, which we evaluate explicitly. From this evaluation we are able to apply the residue theorem and
                                                   find the trace of the trivial representation as a residue. We also recover the contribution of the “special”
                                                   representation.

                                             1. Introduction
   Beyond Endoscopy is a strategy proposed by Langlands to prove the general Principle of Functoriality
(see [38], [11], [4], [36]). In his original monograph (see [37]) it was put forward for GL(2, Q). Since then
it has been subsequently explained and refined for general reductive groups (see [5], [6]). However, there
are few concrete cases in which the different stages of the strategy have been successfully carried out.
   The strategy was first carried out successfully in the series of papers [2], [3] and [1] of Altuğ. In
these articles, it is proven that the predictions of Langlands for Beyond Endoscopy indeed hold for a
certain particular case. Based on Altuğ’s work, other papers generalized his results to other cases where
certain assumptions were changed. For example, for more general number fields see [24]; for allowing extra
ramification see [15]. Importantly, one must also mention the work [47], where the Kuznetsov Trace Formula
is used, instead of the Arthur-Selberg Trace formula, to carry out the ideas put forward by Langlands.
Ultimately, one would desire to deduce from the strategy of Beyond Endoscopy results similar to those of
[47]. All the previous articles work in GL(2).
   The present paper is the first one to successfully carry out part of the strategy in a new group. We
generalize to GL(3, Q) the results of [2] under the assumption of a specific conjecture we explain below.
Concretely, this means that we isolate the contribution of the trivial representation from the geometric side
of the trace formula via the application of Poisson Summation. Our main result is
Theorem A. The following equality holds
                                        1
                 Iell (f ) − Tr(1(f )) = Tr(ξ(f )) + Σ((ξ, η) ̸= (0, 0)) − Σ(f ) + (38) + (40).
                                        3
   The terms on the right-hand side are explicit and will require further study. Here, ξ is the representation
obtained from inducing the trivial representation on the Borel subgroup of GL(3, Q). It is the counterpart
of what Altuğ calls the special representation. The rest of the terms are concrete remnants from Poisson
Summation and the calculus of residues.
   In this introduction, we will explain how the above theorem was obtained and what each of the involved
terms means. We will briefly discuss the strategy of Beyond Endoscopy for GL(n, Q). We will then discuss
our work in GL(3, Q) and, at the same time, compare it with the corresponding steps in GL(2, Q). We
shall also make explicit the conjecture mentioned above.
   At first glance, one can see that the processes for GL(2, Q) and GL(3, Q) resemble each other more than
one might have expected. This can be misleading. The details to carry out the steps for GL(3, Q) are more
complicated and the techniques required are more sophisticated.

1.1. Brief idea of the general strategy. The trace formula is an equality between two distributions,
usually called the geometric side and the spectral side of the trace formula. In turn, each of these sides is
built out of smaller distributions, each of which is built upon, and thus reflects, various properties of the
group and its representation theory. For more careful discussion, we refer the reader to [8], [34], [27].
   Each side of the trace formula has its main part. These are not equal as distributions. For the geometric
side, the main part is the regular elliptic part of the trace formula
                                                      X
                                          Iell (f ) =   vol(γ)O(γ, f ),
                                                   [γ]

where [γ] runs over regular elliptic conjugacy classes in GL(n, Q).
  In the above, f is a test function in Cc (Z+ GL(n, Q)\GL(n, AQ )) where AQ are the adeles of Q and
Z+ ∼
   = R+ is the identity component of the center of GLn (R). We also have vol(γ) which refers to the adelic
                                                                                                            3

volume of the centralizer Gγ of γ, that is
                                                                  
                                     vol(γ) = vol Z+ Gγ (Q)\Gγ (A) ,
and                                            Z
                                  O(γ, f ) =                        f (x−1 γx)dx,
                                               Z+ Gγ (AQ )\G(AQ )

is the orbital integral of f . The measure dx must be chosen carefully, but we will leave it unspecified
during the introduction.
   The main term on the spectral side is the discrete part of the trace formula, which is a sum
                                             X
                                                 mπ Tr(π(f )),
                                                   π

over the irreducible representations π which appear in L2 (Z+ GL(n, Q)\GL(n, AQ )) with finite multiplicity
mπ . Here, f is again a test function in the relevant Schwartz space and π(f ) is the corresponding operator.
   Associated to each automorphic representation π of GL(n, Q) and any finite dimensional algebraic rep-
resentation of the dual group
                                          r : GL(n, C) → GL(N, C),
we expect to construct an automorphic L−function L(s, π, r). Define µπ,r as the order of the pole at s = 1
of L(s, π, r).
   The strategy of Beyond Endoscopy stipulates that there must exist an r−trace formula for each choice
of r. That is, when r is fixed, there must exist two distributions, which we will call the geometric side and
the spectral side of the r−trace formula, that should be equal. In turn, each of these distributions would
be built out of smaller distributions themselves. Furthermore, when r is the trivial representation, this
r−trace formula should be the usual stable trace formula. In the case at hand the stable trace formula is
the invariant trace formula.
   These constructions are highly speculative. The desire to build such a family of trace formulas comes
from the possibility to organize automorphic representations according to the behavior of poles of L(s, π, r)
as r varies. This organization leads, if carried out correctly, to very deep consequences associated to the
principle of functoriality and its potential proof (see [5]).
   Analogous to the stable trace formula, each side of the r-trace formula should have its main term.
Langlands suggests in [37] that on the spectral side of the r−trace formula, the main part should be
                                             X
(1)                                              µπ,r mπ Tr(π(f )).
                                               π

Arthur refers to this as the discrete part of the r−trace formula.
   However, that these orders µπ,r exist (i.e. that the poles exist) is a consequence of the Principle of
Functoriality. Thus, we are unable to use them as concrete objects because their existence and behavior
remains unproven. We need an expression that does not rely on Functoriality for its definition. Further-
more, it is not clear how the main geometric part (i.e. the regular elliptic part) should be defined for this
conjectural r−trace formula.
   Langlands proves that associated to a test function f , there is a family of adelic test functions f p ,
parametrized by the primes p of Q, such that (1) becomes
                                           1       X         X
(2)                             lim                   log(p)   mπ Tr(π(f p )).
                               n→∞ #{p | p ≤ n}
                                                       p≤n   π
4                                     TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

The construction of this family of functions is very concrete. Briefly, one multiplies the p-factor of f by
the basic function associated to r. We refer the reader to [5] or [14] for a discussion of its construction.
Notice that (2) does not depend on functoriality any longer.
   The above is an averaging involving discrete parts of the trace formula for the functions f p . Thus, using
our presupposed equality with the regular elliptic part, the above should be equal to
                                        1        X         X
(3)                           lim                   log(p)      vol(γ)O(γ, f p ).
                             n→∞ #{p | p ≤ n}
                                                  p≤n          [γ]

   Both limits, in equations (2) and (3), make sense without functoriality. However, due to the presence
of nontempered representations in the discrete part of the trace formula, the spectral side limit will not
converge.
   In the situation we will be interested in, the only such non-tempered representations is the trivial one.
For simplicity of exposition, let us suppose only the trivial representation appears in the residual part of
the discrete spectrum. We could isolate it from the spectral sum and merge it with the geometric side
limit. By doing this, we get the equality between
                                                                                       
                                  1      X            X
(4)                    lim                    log(p)     vol(γ)O(γ, f p ) − Tr(1(f p ))
                      n→∞ #{p | p ≤ n}
                                           p≤n           [γ]

and
                                         1      X        X
                                lim               log(p)   mπ Tr(π(f p ))
                               n→∞ #{p | p ≤ n}
                                                   p≤n         π̸=1
What remains in the discrete spectral side should converge since we have taken away the nontempered
terms. Thus, what is on the geometric side must converge as well. We conclude that there should be a way
to manipulate the regular elliptic part that isolates the trace of the trivial representation and cancels it, so
that what remains when plugged into the limit converges to what the rest of the spectral side limit is.
   If the above succeeds, then we will have evidence of the existence of two different distributions which
we might propose are the main parts of our geometric and spectral sides of the r−trace formula. As we
see from the above explanation, our main result precisely gives us a concrete expansion of the term
                                       X
(5)                                        vol(γ)O(γ, f p ) − Tr(1(f p ))
                                         [γ]

which can be substituted into the limit. The study of the limit and its convergence is work in progress.
   In the above explanation, we have followed the calculations in the original monograph [37]. It was pointed
out by Sarnak in [42] that there is a significant difference between considering averages over primes p and
averages over integers m. This is equivalent to weighting the discrete part of the trace formula with the
order µπ,r , as we have done in (1) above, or weighting with the residue itself. He explains that the original
choice would lead to very complicated estimation required for the computations of the limits. In his work
[1], Altuğ works with the latter choice.
   For the purposes of concrete manipulation, the difference manifests itself in the number of places the
function f is modified simultaneously. In the present work we modify the function in a single fixed prime
p, following [2]. A close inspection of all the results and their proofs shows that they hold if instead we
modified a finite number of primes at the same time. That is, the results of this paper for general m follow
from those at primes p. However, for subsequent work where estimates and limits must be calculated one
needs to work with m.
                                                                                                            5

   What remains to be discussed is the way in which one intends to actually study the difference (5). The
regular elliptic part of the trace formula is indexed by the regular elliptic conjugacy classes of GL(n, Q).
These, in turn, are characterized by their characteristic polynomial
                             X n − an−1 X n−1 + · · · + (−1)n−1 a1 X + (−1)n a0 .
In [25] it was put forward that one might attempt to perform the isolation process by performing a Poisson
Summation on the coefficients (an−1 , ..., a1 , a0 ) that parametrize these conjugacy classes. However, not
every choice of coefficients correspond to a regular elliptic class. Only those that give rise to irreducible
polynomials over Q parametrize such a class. Thus, before any such summation can be performed, one must
understand explicitly how the individual terms of the regular elliptic part depend on these coefficients. One
does this with the hope of extending their values to the missing points, in such a way that the summation
becomes possible. In this paper we follow this philosophy to study (5).
1.2. The case of GL(3, Q). It was put forward in [24] that the strategy of isolation seems to be naturally
divided into five stages. We explain our contributions following these five steps. For this part, we will
suppose the reader is familiar with the concrete strategy as carried out in [2] and, at some points, in [24].
   In all our work we fix a prime p, as explained above, and a varying prime q which can be equal to p.
The only exception made is in the computations of the Kloosterman sums where we assumed p ̸= 2, 3.
1.2.1. Step 1: Rewriting the regular elliptic part. The regular elliptic conjugacy classes for GL(3, Q) are
parametrized by their characteristic polynomial X 3 − aX 2 + bX − c. The choice of test function implies
c = ±pk for a fixed positive integer k. Consequently, the sum runs over two integral parameters a and b,
such that the given cubic polynomial is irreducible over Q. The conjugacy class associated to it is denoted
by γ(a, b). This cubic polynomial also generates a cubic number field, denoted E(a, b), and an order R(a, b)
inside of it. This order is the one generated by the root of the polynomial and might not coincide with the
ring of integers of E(a, b).
   The regular elliptic part Iell is a sum of terms
                                                                                 Y
                   vol(γ(a, b))O(γ(a, b), f ) = vol(γ(a, b)) · O(γ(a, b), f∞ ) ·   O(γ(a, b), fq ).
                                                                               q

We need a formula for this term as a function in (a, b) which is suitable to apply the Poisson Summation
formula. The main tool to do their rewriting is the introduction of the following zeta function:
Definition A. Let R0 be a Z-order inside a maximal order O. We define

above, hR (s) and LR (s) are certain functions of a complex parameter defined in Subsection 4.3.2. The first
named author was led to this definition by his work [21], where he and coauthor studied a conjecture by
Cherednik-Danilenko-Philipp relating Yun’s zeta function to motivic superpolynomials(cf. [21, Conjecture
3.38], [17] and [18]).

Zagier, in [49], introduced for each δ ≡ 0, 1 (mod 4) the following function
                                                              

The ′ in the sum means that it is taken only over f such that δ/f 2 is itself congruent to 0 or 1 modulo 4.
It has come to be known in the literature as Zagier’s zeta function. Since its introduction, it has found
many applications (e.g. see [12], [44]). In particular, it is known that this zeta function can be completed
to one that satisfies a functional equation.
   What Altuğ realizes is that for a regular elliptic class γ, in GL(2, Q), one has
                                     O(s, γ, f ) = Z(s, Tr(γ)2 − 4 det(γ)).
In this way he introduces a function Z that satisfies three properties:
    (1) At s = 1 this function recovers the value of the product of the finite orbital integrals,
    (2) It satisfies a functional equation,
    (3) It has an explicit Dirichlet series and, if one expands this function as a Dirichlet series, then its
        coefficients and its indexing set make sense for parameters that do not come from regular elliptic
        classes.
The above three properties are what makes the whole strategy to work. Indeed, (1) tells us that we can
substitute, in the regular elliptic part, each finite product of orbital integrals for the evaluation at s = 1 of
these zeta functions. Then (2) will allow us to perform the Approximate Functional Equation (which we
discuss in the next step). Finally, (3) allows us to complete the lattice on which we must perform Poisson
Summation. It is now clear that if one wishes to generalize the work of Altuğ to more general groups, one
needs a generalization of Zagier’s zeta function for GL(n, Q).
   In [48], Yun defines for a given order R (global or local) a zeta function JR (s). He verifies that it has
a functional equation. Furthermore, he proves that if one picks as order R = Zq [γ], (that is, the order
generated by γ within the extension generated by its characteristic polynomial over Zq ) then one recovers
the value of the q-adic orbital integral we have considered above. This makes Yun’s zeta functions prime
candidates to play the role for GL(n, Q) that Zagier’s zeta function played for GL(2, Q). In [7], Arthur
proposed that for GL(2, Q) they were in fact the same function. This was verified in [22] and [23]. In the
                                                                                                                7

main results of [13], Yun’s zeta functions are applied to obtain the leading term in counting lattice points
in a fixed elliptic orbit in GL(n) over number fields.
   As a consequence, for GL(2, Q) we have several constructions that we know produce the same function of
                                                                  J    (s)
a complex parameter: O(s, γ, f ), Z(s, Tr(γ)2 − 4 det(γ)) and ζZ[γ] Q (s)

they are the same, is more suited to show why the function satisfies the properties (1), (2) and (3) we

                                       proves (1) and (2). However, (3) is provided to us by Z(s, δ).
   The definition A will play for us the role that Z(s, δ) plays for GL(2, Q). That is, it will give us Dirichlet
series whose coefficients we can manipulate concretely, both locally and globally, and that can be extended
to the missing parameters. However, we were unable to successfully prove that it coincides with Yun’s
zeta function. Nevertheless, in all cases where we can compute both functions, they coincide. We thus put
forward the following
Conjecture A. Let R be a Gorenstein order (finite rank algebra) over Z. Let R∨ = Hom(R, Z) be its
dual. Let                                    X

be Yun’s zeta function [48]. Then we have

   One can easily verify the conjecture for GL(2). In GL(3), in principle this can be derived from our
computations in the appendix. In addition, we expect modifications for GL(n) for n > 3.
   Without assuming the conjecture we are able to verify that at s = 1 we recover the orbital integral. We
need to assume the conjecture to deduce the functional equation. In particular, with these definitions and
assumptions, we can write the regular elliptic part as follows

1.2.3. Step 3: Problem of completion and Poisson Summation. The goal is to perform Poisson Summation
over (a, b). As we have said before, only the parameters that give conjugacy classes that are regular elliptic
must be included. However, to perform the summation we must add back the excluded ones for otherwise
our lattice is incomplete. In our case, everything that we are defining makes sense for the remaining
parameters as long as we interpret R(a, b) as the order associated to the cubic form
                                        X 3 − aX 2 Y + bXY 2 ∓ pk Y 3 ,
via the classification of cubic orders via binary cubic forms. We refer the reader to [26, Proposition 4.2]
for more on this classification. This classification dates back to [20].
   Under that assumption, the sum
                                             ∞
        XX X                                 X  WR (n)  ±                                                   
                           aR (d)[R : Rγ ]−1             Φnd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b) + nd[R : Rγ ]2 Ψ±
                                                                                            nd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b)
                                                   n
       (a,b) d|sγ Rγ ⊂R⊂OE                n=1

now makes sense for every (a, b). The added terms (i.e. those that did not come from a regular elliptic
class) is what we have called Σ(f ) in the statement of Theorem A above.
   The next stage is to isolate the smooth functions, i.e. to find a periodicity in the integer coefficients
because these are not the evaluation of a smooth functions on a lattice. We prove
Theorem B. Fix positive integers f , n and d|n. Then, the value of the sum
                                         X
                                                aR (d)WR (n/d)
                                          [R:R(a,b)]=f

depends only on (a, b) modulo nf 2 .
  This theorem is proven in Section 8. It reduces to a local proof in which one verifies that both coefficients
depend on the structure of the ring R/(nf 2 )R. We want to emphasize that even though we do not see
the serious machinations of commutative algebra in the statements of our theorems, they are very much in
                                                                                                                                                                       9

there. This is an interesting contrast with the case for GL(2) where commutative algebra does not really
play a significant role.

                                             f 2 |m2 ±4p   k   l=1


From here, the periodicity in m is obvious because it is the one of the Kronecker Symbol
                                                            !
                                            (m2 ± 4pk )/f 2
                                                              ,
                                                   l

i.e. periodic in m, the trace variable, modulo 4lf 2 . Similarly, for GL(2, K) in [24], at this stage one has
(in their notation)
                                                                                   
                       X      X XX                     Φ± (τ, u)
                                           χd (a)                  + NK (d)Ψ± (τ, u) .
                          ∗ 2            a
                                                    NK (d)NK (a)
                             u∈OK τ −4uε̸=□ d|sγ

One has to make sense of the missing terms, but this time they are not obvious, because they are implicit in
the formulas. However, unlike our situation, these terms are not a priori defined for the general setting. In
[24] the authors overcome this by introducing (1) explicit congruence conditions, instead of the divisibility
condition and (2) the explicit restricted modified Hilbert Symbol, which allows them to add the extra
terms (this is necessary because there is no corresponding Kronecker Symbol for general number fields).
Once this is done, once more the periodicity follows from that of the symbol.
   Our current situation is a mix of the above. We have been able to define the terms in general from
the start (i.e. Definition A holds for all cubic orders). Furthermore, the indexing sum goes over orders,
as opposed to integers (which play the role of the order conductors). With this we avoid the explicit
divisibility condition and congruence conditions. The difficulty they impose is still there, but it manifests
in the behavior of the coefficients and, consequently, in the evaluation of the Kloosterman sums.
   One might be tempted to attempt to write the coefficients of these zeta functions in terms of reciprocity
symbols. This seems to be unwise because prime splitting is difficult to describe in a uniform way through
different cubic fields. Indeed, for quadratic fields it requires only the quadratic Hecke character and its
manifestations as Hilbert and Kronecker symbols. For cubic fields, on the other hand, it depends on which
type of field it is. If it is a Galois cubic field, Hecke characters are enough; if it is cubic and non-Galois,
one needs both cubic and quadratic characters associated to the Galois closure. Consequently, it does not
seem possible to write uniform formulas from which the extension and periodicity can be read off.
   The periodicity our method deduces is nf 2 . Since our computations also apply for GL(2), this implies
that this period should also be possible in the computations carried out in [2], instead of the 4nf 2 used
originally. We verify this is the case, see Subsection 7.3. In this way, now all primes have the same definition
for their Kloosterman sums. In the same direction, we emphasize that the use of overorders has allowed us
to avoid finding congruence conditions, which were also a source of disagreement between the definitions
at odd and even primes for the Kloosterman sums.
10                                          TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

  Once this periodicity is established, the sum over (a, b) gets broken into sublattices modulo nf 2 . Poisson
Summation is performed in each sublattice. The traces we are looking for are located in the main term of
the spectral side of the Poisson Summation. This is

The term Σ((ξ, η) ̸= (0, 0)) in Theorem A refers to the spectral terms of the Poisson Summation that are
not the main ones.
1.2.4. Step 4: Concrete evaluation of Kloosterman-type sums. From the last manipulation, one defines
Kloosterman-type sums and their Dirichlet series. The first definition is
Definition B. Let n, f be fixed integers and ± a fixed sign. We define the Kloosterman-Type sum as
                                          X          X       X
                        K ± (n, f ) =                          daR (d)WR (n/d)
                                               (a,b)   mod nf 2 [R:R(a,b)]=f d|n

   We see that, contrary to what happens in GL(2, Q), this time the coefficients are implicitly described by
the behavior of aR (d) and WR (n). The study of these coefficients is necessary for the explicit evaluation
of these sums.
   Associated to the above Kloosterman-type sums we have the following
Definition C. Fix a sign ±. We define the double Dirichlet series
                                                             ∞ X
                                                               ∞
                                                             X   K ± (n, f )
                                                D± (z) =                              .
                                                                        nz+2 f 2z+3
                                                             n=1 f =1

   This definition might look “ad-hoc” but it is not. The calculus of residues of the final step forces this
choice of definition. It is remarkable how similar they are to those of GL(2). Indeed, for GL(2, Q) the
exponents of n and f are, respectively, z + 1 and 2z + 1. Of course, the major difference is on the values
of the coefficients K ± (n, f ).
   One of the main results of this work (and one could argue the main one) is the successful evaluation of
this sum. We have
Theorem C. For a complex parameter z with ℜ(z) > 1, we have
                                         ζ(2z)     ζ(3z)     1 − p−z(k+1) 1 − p−z(k+2)
                           D± (z) = ·           ·          ·             ·
                                        ζ(z + 1) ζ(2z + 1)     1 − p−z      1 − p−2z
In particular, D± (z) is independent of the sign ± and admits a meromorphic continuation to the whole
complex plane.
   Let us briefly discuss the proof of the theorem. The first step is to prove that the Kloosterman sums
K ± (n, f ) admit an Euler product decomposition. This is proven in Proposition 27. From this it immedi-
ately follows that D± (z) also admits an Euler factor decomposition. Consequently, everything reduces to
computing explicitly the local factors of the Kloosterman sums. This is done in Section 8. It involves two
steps:
                                                                                                           11

   (1) If our order R(a, b) is produced by the binary cubic form f , then we explicitly describe the integral
       overorders, at a fixed index. Notice this is the indexing set of the middle sum in the definition
       of K ± (n, f ). To do this we use the well known GL(2, Z)-action on binary cubic forms f 7→ f g .
       This action was first exploited by Shintani [43] as an application of the theory of zeta functions
       on prehomogeneous spaces and derived interesting results on class numbers of cubic fields. Many
       authors have revisited this action since then (e.g. [19], [10]).
          We describe a condition on g for this to be an integral overorder. These are the only orders that
       contribute to the sums via their coefficients aR (d)WR (n). This is the content of Proposition 30.
   (2) The condition found in the first part translates to a system of congruences (i.e. System (36)). This
       system depends on certain parameters. We write down the explicit system of congruences for each
       of the necessary cases. This will allow us to count their solutions and produce the corresponding
       orders and their contributions to the sums. This is done in Theorem 5.
   The above two steps produce solutions which construct overorders. Moreover, the construction is done
in such a way that we can evaluate WR (n) and aR (d) from them. Collecting these values together ap-
propriately is what leads to the explicit evaluations. The work in the appendices carries out the explicit
division in cases and the counting and organization of solutions. All the different cases, and where they
are handled, are summarized in Table 1.

                         (q, 6p) = 1 q = p, (p, 6) = 1 q = 2, p ̸= 2 q = 3, p ̸= 3
                        Appendix A      Appendix B     Appendix C Appendix D
                                  Table 1. Kloosterman cases calculation


   In the Appendices C and D we have only given the proof for k = 1. For larger values of k the computations
increase in size, as the number of cases grows, without any new insight on why the final result should be
true. Furthermore, for subsequent work (i.e. analyzing the limit) the current analytical tools probably can
only handle the case k = 1. See [3] and [1]; they restrict to k = 1 precisely for this reason.
   We recall that we have assumed in all our computations that p ̸= 2, 3. The corresponding computations
and final answers are the same, but we leave those cases to the interested reader. Upon seeing the appendices
the process to follow is similar.
   We emphasize here, and do it again in the appendices, that we unfortunately flipped the use of p and q.
The notation in Table 1 is in accordance with the main text. Once the reader goes through the appendices,
we hope they sympathize with our choice of not flipping them back to avoid unnecessary typos in an
already complicated calculation.

1.2.5. Step 5: Isolation of the trivial representation from the discrete spectrum. Once we have succeeded
in calculating the Kloosterman sums and their Dirichlet series, we are able to conclude. The sum we need
to analyze gets broken down into two sums.
Definition D. We define the sums

  The first sum will give the trace of the trivial representation while the second one will give the special
one ξ. We have not made it explicit in our current discussion, but both Φ±                 ±
                                                                                  n,f and Ψn,f are defined via
Mellin transforms. Consequently, they really are the line integral of some complex functions introduced in
the approximate functional equation step. When one opens the definitions, one can shift the contours of
the complex integral. For example, one calculates that
                       XZ Z
                 R1 =             θ± (xp−k/3 , yp−2k/3 )Fe(z)|Pol(x, y)|αz D± (z + 1)dxdydz.
                           ±   σ   R2

All the terms we have not explained so far are carefully introduced in the text. For the moment, let us
focus on how we use our calculations. Theorem C allows us to specifically know the poles and residues
of D± (z + 1). In particular, we can shift the contour to 0 > σ > −1/2 and only catch the residue at
z = 0. That residue is the trace of the trivial representation. One knows this because we have calculated
independently that value. The line integral that remains is what we have called (38) in Theorem A above.
An analogous analysis shows that the residue at z = 0 of R2 gives the trace of ξ plus a new contour integral,
which we call (40) in Theorem A.

                                               2. The basic setup
  We begin our work by making choices that will be constant throughout the work and drawing conse-
quences of these choices.

2.0.1. The test function. For the rest of the paper we fix a prime p and an integer k ≥ 0. We shall reserve
the letter q to denote a general finite prime that, unless otherwise stated, can be equal to p.

2.0.2. Regular elliptic elements, the discriminant and number theory. We recall that for GL(3) we have
the following definition.
Definition 2. Let γ ∈ GL(3, Q). We say that γ is a regular elliptic element if its characteristic polynomial
                                            X 3 − aX 2 + bX − c,
is irreducible over Q. We say it is regular if its characteristic polynomial has no repeated roots over Q.
Remark 2. The definition of regular elliptic given above works for GL(n) over any number field. For
more general groups one must use more general definitions.
  Associated to a cubic polynomial X 3 − aX 2 + bX − c we define its discriminant. Concretely, we have
Definition 3. Let X 3 − aX 2 + bX − c be a cubic polynomial with roots λ1 , λ2 , λ3 , then its discriminant is
defined as
                                   ((λ1 − λ2 )(λ2 − λ3 )(λ3 − λ1 ))2 .
  The following are well known properties of the discriminant for the cubic case.
Proposition 1. Let X 3 − aX 2 + bX − c be a cubic polynomial. Then
   (1) The discriminant is a polynomial in a, b, c. Concretely,
                              Pol(a, b, c) := a2 b2 − 4b3 − 4a3 c + 18abc − 27c2 .
   (2) For r > 0, the discriminant satisfies
                                      Pol(ra, r2 b, r3 c) = r6 Pol(a, b, c).
   (3) The polynomial has repeated roots if and only if Pol(a, b, c) = 0.
   (4) The polynomial has three different real roots if and only if Pol(a, b, c) > 0. Consequently, it has
       exactly one real root if and only if Pol(a, b, c) < 0.
  Great part of the manipulations that we will do have certain number-theoretical source associated to
the following data.
Definition 4. Let γ be a regular elliptic element with characteristic polynomial X 3 − aX 2 + bX − c. We
define
                                                         Q[X]
                                         Eγ :=                         ,
                                               (X − aX 2 + bX − c)
                                                   3

the cubic number field generated by γ. We identify γ with the root (X).
   Given a number field E, we denote by DE = DE/Q the discriminant of this extension, by hE its class
number, RE its regulator, and ωE its number of roots of unity. If this number field is produced by the
characteristic polynomial of a regular elliptic element γ we write instead Dγ , hγ , Rγ , and ωγ .
   Finally, the ring of integers of the number field will be denoted by OE . We instead use Oγ if this number
field is produced by the characteristic polynomial of a regular elliptic element γ.
2.0.3. Measures and orbital integrals. We now explain our choices of measures. We begin by measures
that do not involve a choice of regular elliptic element. At a non-archimedean place q, we take the Haar
measure on GL(3, Qq ) giving GL(3, Zq ) volume 1. At infinity, we pick the standard Lebesgue measure. In
Z+ we put the Haar measure dz/z. Finally, GL(3, A) has the associated restricted product measure.
   We now explain measures that involve a regular elliptic element γ ∈ GL(3, Q). Denote by Tγ the
centralizer of γ in GL(3). We normalize the Haar measure
                                                      Y
                                               d× x =    d× xv
                                                         v
14                                 TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

on Tγ (A) as follows:
                                                                                           ×
     • for each finite place q, d× xq is normalized by vol Tγ (Zq ) = 1, where Tγ (Zq ) = OE,q
                                                                   
                                                                                               ;
                             ×
     • for each real place, d x = dx/|x| on R ; ×

     • for each complex place, d× z = 2 dx dy/|z|2 on C× .
  The above choices define the (finite) local orbital integral

                                              Tγ (Qq )\GL(3,Qq )

elliptic over Q, but it might not be over Qq . For us the following result will be fundamental.
Proposition 2. Let γ be a regular semi-simple element in GL(3, Qq ), q be a prime and r ≥ 0 be an integer.
If | det(γ)|q ̸= q −r , then
                                                 O(γ, χq,r ) = 0.
Consequently, if | det(γ)|q ̸= q −k q then O(γ, fq ) = 0, where
                                                    (

                                            kq :=
                                                      k,    if q = p.
Proof. If O(γ, χq,r ) ̸= 0, then a conjugate of γ must be in the support of χq,r . Since the determinant is
invariant under conjugation, the result follows. Furthermore, because the test function fq at the prime q
is a constant multiple of χq,rq with rq = 0 for q ̸= p and rp = k, the same conclusion holds.            □
  As we will see in later sections, we will find a way to avoid evaluating the orbital integrals explicitly.
However, there is one case where we do need to know its value.
Proposition 3. Let q be a prime and r ≥ 0 an integer. Let γ be a regular elliptic element in GL(3, Q)
such that | det(γ)|q = q −r . Suppose that γ splits entirely in Qq with roots λ1 , λ2 and λ3 in Zq . Then
                              O(γ, χq,r ) = |λ1 − λ2 |−1          −1          −1
                                                      q |λ2 − λ3 |q |λ3 − λ1 |q .

Proof. This is obtained by applying [48, Corollary 4.10].                                                 □
2.0.4. Contributing Classes and their properties. For a factorizable function, as is our test function, the
global orbital integral that appears in the trace formula is
                                                             Y
                                      O(γ, f ) = O(γ, f∞ ) ·   O(γ, fq ).
                                                                   q
Proposition 2 above implies conditions on the regular elliptic classes for which the above orbital integral
doesn’t vanish.
Proposition 4. Let γ ∈ GL(3, Q) be a regular elliptic matrix. If
                                                 O(γ, f ) ̸= 0,
then det(γ) = ±pk . Furthermore, in this case the characteristic polynomial of γ is in Z[X].
Proof. Let q be a prime. Given that the global orbital integral does not vanish, we know O(γ, fq ) ̸= 0.
This implies that a conjugate of γ lands in the support of χq,kq , where kq = 0 if q ̸= p and kp = k. By
definition, the support of χq,kq lands inside of Mat(3, Zq ).
  The characteristic polynomial of the matrices in Mat(3, Zq ) have coefficients in Zq . Furthermore, char-
acteristic polynomial is invariant under conjugation. Consequently, the coefficients of the characteristic
                                                                                                            15

polynomial of γ are in Zq . Because this holds for every finite prime q, we conclude the coefficients of the
characteristic polynomials are in Z.
  Finally, Proposition 2 specifies that
                                                     (
                                                      1      q ̸= p,
                                        | det(γ)|q =
                                                      p−k q = p.
This implies that det(γ) = ±pk . This concludes the proof.                                                  □
  The above proposition motivated our following definition.
Definition 5. Let γ be a regular elliptic element in GL(3, Q) with characteristic polynomial X 3 − aX 2 +
bX − c. We say that γ is a contributing conjugacy class if det(γ) = c = ±pk and a, b ∈ Z.
   The above discussion guarantees that the nonzero terms of the regular elliptic part of the trace formula,
for our test function, are within those indexed by classes whose characteristic polynomials are of the form
                                    X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk ,          a, b ∈ Z.
We now set up the following convention.
Notation 1. Let γ be a contributing class with characteristic polynomial X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk . We define
                                         Pol(a, b) := Pol(a, b, ±pk ).
Remark 3. A contributing class is implicitly associated to a choice of sign ±. We shall suppress that
dependence in some of the notation. However, any time a construction that a priori depends on all the
coefficients a, b, c of the characteristic polynomial, appears as a function of a, b alone, is because we have
fixed c as ±pk .

                       3. The Residual Representations and their Traces
3.1. The residual representations that will appear. For the sake of clarity let us begin by elaborating
on which traces we are looking to eliminate via this process.
Proposition 5. In the context of the setup established in the previous section, we have
                                     L2res (Z+ GL(3, Q)\GL(3, AQ )) = 1,
where 1 denotes the trivial representation.
Proof. The discrete spectrum of GL(n) can be decomposed as
                                                           ˆ
                                                          MM
                           L2 (Z + GL(n, Q)\GL(n, AQ )) =    Speh(σ, n/d),
                                                              d|n    σ

where σ runs over the cuspidal spectrum of GL(d, Q) (see [40], [28]). In the above sum, Speh(σ, n/d) is
the Speh representation associated to σ and n/d. Recall that
                                                              k+1
                                                                    
                                   Speh(σ, k) = ⊞ki=1 σ ⊗ | · | 2 −i .
In particular, the case d = n corresponds to the cuspidal spectrum and the rest of the divisors parametrize
the residual spectrum. For the case of interest, which is n = 3, we conclude that we are looking to eliminate
the traces of the term given by d = 1,
                                                                 Mˆ
                               L2res (Z + GL(3, Q)\GL(3, AQ )) =    Speh(σ, 3).
                                                                     σ
16                                       TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

In this situation σ runs over automorphic representations of GL(1, Q). These are precisely the Hecke
characters. Let us now be slightly more specific. For a given Hecke character σ, the Speh representation is
                                                    σ ⊗ | · | ⊞ σ ⊞ σ ⊗ | · |−1 .
At a central element zI3 , the previous representation takes the value
                                           σ(z)|z| × σ(z) × σ(z)|z|−1 = σ 3 (z).
We conclude that the central character of this representation is σ 3 .
   On the other hand, because S = {∞}, we must have representations that are unramified at every finite
place. Speh preserves ramification, that is, Speh(σ, 3) is ramified at exactly the places where σ is. This
implies σ is unramified at every finite place. Furthermore, the central character of these representations
must be trivial, because Q has class number 1. This implies that σ 3 = 1 and σ is unramified at every finite
place. The only character that satisfies these conditions is σ = 1. Indeed, by Class Field Theory, any such
character would correspond to an unramified cubic extension of Q and these extensions do not exist due
to Cl(Q) = 1. In conclusion,
                                    L2res (Z+ GL(3, Q)\GL(3, AQ )) = Speh(1, 3) = 1.
                                                                                                                     □

3.3. The trace of the trivial representation at the infinite place. It will be important for us to
rewrite the Archimedean orbital integral as well. We recall that given a regular element γ the Archimedean
part of the trace integral is                 Z
                                  O(γ, f∞ ) =              f∞ (x−1 γx)dx.
                                                     Z+ \GL(3,R)
This function is invariant under conjugation, which is to say, that it only depends on the conjugacy class
of γ. For purposes of our manipulation, we shall require the Weyl Integration formula for Z+ \GL(3, R).
We now make the required preparations.
Proposition 7.       (1) The group GL(3, R) has two families of maximal torus: the split family torus Ts
       and a family of mixed torus Tm . The corresponding Weyl Groups cardinalities are |W (Ts , R)| = 6
       and |W (Tm , R)| = 2.
   (2) The group GL(3, R) has two families of maximal torus: the split family torus Z+ \Ts and a family
       of mixed torus Z+ \Tm . The corresponding Weyl Groups cardinalities are |W (Z+ \Ts , R)| = 6 and
       |W (Z+ \Tm , R)| = 2.
Proof. Part (a) follows from definition and by noticing that in the split torus there are 6 ways to permute
the roots, while on the mixed torus only the complex pair can be permuted. Part (b) follows from part (a)
because Z+ is a subgroup of every maximal torus.                                                         □
  In our setup, we know that f∞ ∈ Cc (Z+ \GL(3, R)) and that its orbital integrals have compact support.
Associated to this function and to each of the maximal tori families there is a corresponding orbital integral.
Concretely, for a maximal torus T of GL(3, R), we define
                                               Z
                                      ΦT (t) =           f∞ (xtx−1 )dx.
                                                    T \GL(3,R)
Using this notation, the Weyl integration formula for Z+ \GL(3, R)
                 Z                        Z                        Z

                              f (g)dg =         ΦT (t)|D(t)|dt +         ΦT (t)|D(t)|dt.
                  Z+ \GL(3,R)           6 Z+ \Ts s               2 Z+ \Tm m
We are supposing that the measures here are normalized as usual to satisfy the Weyl Integration formula
and the integration by stages formula. At this stage, each of the involved integrals is the coefficient on the
torus and not in the space of coefficients of the characteristic polynomial. We now change the integration
space.
   We need concrete representatives of our two families of tori. We specify them through their eigenvalues.
We set
                                     Ts = {diag(λ1 , λ2 , λ3 ) | λi ̸= 0},
                                    Tm = {diag(λ, reiθ , re−iθ ) | λ ̸= 0, r > 0}
     The space of coefficients is given by
                    {(c1 , c2 , c3 ) ∈ R3 | X 3 − c1 X 2 + c2 X − c3 has no repeated roots} ⊂ R3 .
This is an open set given as the complement of Pol(c1 , c2 , c3 ) = 0 and with c3 ̸= 0. The coefficient maps
are given as follows. For the split torus Ts we have
                                             c1 = λ1 + λ2 + λ3 ,
                                             c2 = λ1 λ2 + λ2 λ3 + λ3 λ1 ,
                                                                                                              19

                                           c3 = λ1 λ2 λ3 .
while, for the mixed torus Tm , we have
                                              c1 = λ + 2r cos(θ),
                                              c2 = 2λr cos(θ) + r2 ,
                                              c3 = λr2 .
The Weyl Integration formula assigns for each tori the measure D(t)dt. Despite the notation, both the
Weyl Discriminant and measure depend on the torus T . In coefficient space we have the standard Lebesgue
measure dc := dc1 dc2 dc3 .
Proposition 8. For each maximal tori T we have the following equality of measures
                                  |D(t)| dt = |W (T )| · |Pol(c1 , c2 , c3 )|1/2 dc.
Proof. This is an exercise on differential form manipulations together with the observation that the map
to coefficient space is a |W (T )|-to-1 covering map.                                                  □
  The computations above do not take into account the invariance under the center. To now take this into
account, we must restrict on both parts correspondingly. In order to single out a unique representative
under the action of Z+ we impose the condition that the determinant has absolute value 1. That is,
determinant will be 1 or −1. In other words, in coefficient space, we shall now work in
                    {(c1 , c2 ) ∈ R2 | X 3 − c1 X 2 + c2 X ∓ 1 has no repeated roots} ⊂ R2 .
We are identifying this set with a subset of the hyperplanes (c1 , c2 , ±1) which are parallel to the coordinate
planes. Thus the Lebesgue measure in them is still dc1 dc2 .
   Similarly, for Ts and Tm the determinant condition implies, respectively
                                                   λ1 λ2 λ3 = ±1
                                                         λr2 = ±1.
Under these conditions, by imposing c3 = ±1, we have
Proposition 9. For each maximal tori Z+ \T we have the following equality of measures
                                  |D(t)| dt = |W (T )||Pol(c1 , c2 , ±1)|1/2 dc,
Proof. This follows from Proposition 8 by imposing the determinant conditions.                                □

        4. The manipulation of the regular elliptic part: p-adic Orbital Integrals
4.1. The singular set and the missing lattice points. The regular elliptic part of the trace formula
for GL(3, Q) is defined as             X
                                          vol(γ)O(γ, f ),
                                               [γ]
where the index runs over conjugacy classes of GL(3, Q) which are regular elliptic. As we have seen in
Proposition 4, the characteristic polynomials that parametrize a conjugacy class that contribute to the
sum are of the form
                                           X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk ,
as long as this polynomial is irreducible over Q.
Notation 2. Whenever X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk is irreducible over Q, we shall denote by γ(a, b) the unique
regular elliptic conjugacy class with this characteristic polynomial.
   The above discussion allows us to rewrite the sum over the conjugacy classes as a sum over the pairs
(a, b) and signs ± that produce regular elliptic elements. That is,
                         X                   X X
                            vol(γ)O(γ, f ) =               vol(γ(a, b))O(γ(a, b), f ),
                          [γ]                   ± (a,b)∈V (±)

where V (±) is the set of integral (a, b) such that X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk is regular elliptic. When there is no
risk of confusion we shall simply write (a, b) ∈ V . We shall also write γ instead of γ(a, b) unless we require
to emphasize the parameters.
Remark 5. For GL(3) there is no criterion for irreducibility of a polynomial over Q associated to the
value of the discriminant P ol(a, b, c). In particular, contrary to what happens in GL(2), P ol(a, b, c) ̸= 0
implies regularity but not ellipticity. A criterion for irreducibility, however, is given by the rational root
test: X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk is irreducible over Q if and only if ±pt is not a root for t = 0, ..., k. These are
a finite number of linear equations in (a, b). In particular, this implies
                  V (+) = {(a, b) ∈ Z2 | (±p)3t − a(±p)2t + b(±p) − pk ̸= 0, t = 0, 1, ..., k},
                  V (−) = {(a, b) ∈ Z2 | (±p)3t − a(±p)2t + b(±p) + pk ̸= 0, t = 0, 1, ..., k}.
Example 1. Let us take p = 5, k = 1 and the sign +. This means we are considering integral pairs (a, b)
such that
                                         X 3 − aX 2 + bX − 5,
is irreducible over Q. Remark 5 above implies that the only rational roots this polynomial can have are
1, −1, 5 and −5. Substituting each of these roots instead of X and simplifying powers of 5, we get the
following four lines:
(6)                                                  a − b + 4 = 0,
(7)                                                  a + b + 6 = 0,
(8)                                            5a − b − 24 = 0,
(9)                                            5a + b + 26 = 0.
Respectively, these lines correspond to 1, −1, 5 and −5 being a root of the corresponding polynomial.
Each of these lines has an infinite number of lattice points. Consequently, each of those points will not
parametrize a regular elliptic class.
24                                 TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA




                                          Figure 1. Plane c = 5


   In Figure 1 we see several things. The plane we are showing corresponds to c = 5. Notice that this
plane is not D+ because we have not imposed the determinant to be 1. In an analysis with the choices
p = 5 and k = 1 there is another plane, which we are not showing, where the sign is − and the analyzed
polynomial is X 3 − aX 2 + bX + 5. To see that corresponding plane see Figure 2.
   The four black lines correspond to the linear equations (6) to (9). The red boundary that separates the
green and blue regions correspond to the equation

                                              Pol(a, b, 5) = 0,

which is a cubic equation in two variables a, b. The blue region corresponds to Pol(a, b, 5) < 0. It corre-
sponds to points that parametrize regular elliptic classes whose centralizer is of mixed type (i.e. conjugate
to Tm ). The green region corresponds to Pol(a, b, 5) > 0. It corresponds to points that parametrize regular
elliptic classes whose centralizer is split (i.e. conjugate to Ts ).
   Here we can see several types of missing lattice points. There are missing points on the singular set. For
example, (3, −9) and (7, 11) are such points. The polynomials to which they correspond are, respectively,

                                  X 3 − 3X 2 − 9X − 5 = (X + 1)2 (X − 5),
                                 X 3 − 7X 2 + 11X − 5 = (X − 1)2 (X − 5).

We see both of these points induce polynomials with repeated roots as expected. We also have points
missing outside the singular set. For example, (4, −4) corresponds to

                               X 3 − 4X 2 − 4X − 5 = (X − 5)(X 2 + X + 1),
                                                                                                           25

which indeed has exactly one real rational root. On the other hand, we have, for example, the point
(−5, −1) which lies in the lines (6), (7) and (9). Indeed we have it corresponds to
                              X 3 + 5X 2 − X − 5 = (X + 1)(X − 1)(X + 5).
Finally, an example of a point that does parametrize a regular elliptic class is (−2, 7). It corresponds to
the polynomial
                                          X 3 + 2X 2 + 7X − 5.
It lies outside the four lines and thus it has no rational roots. Furthermore, because it lies in the blue
region (equivalently, Pol(−2, 7, 5) < 0) we know that centralizer of the conjugacy class it parametrizes is a
mixed maximal torus. We shall return to the points of this example as we move along.                       □

Remark 6. With our chosen measure normalizations one has, in terms of the class number hγ , regulator
Rγ , and number of roots of unity ωγ of the field E = Eγ = Q[x]/(Pγ (x)) with discriminant DE/Q ,
                                                       2r1 (2π)r2 hγ Rγ
(10)                                        vol(γ) =
                                                              wγ
where r1 (resp. r2 ) is the number of real places (resp. complex places).
Remark 7. The extension Eγ /Q can have different types of Galois group for different (a, b). There are two
possibilities: either the extension is a cyclic Galois extension or the Galois closure is a degree six extension
whose Galois group is S3 . Concretely, we have P (a, b) is a square if and only if the cubic extension Eγ /Q
is Galois. Furthermore, the extension is totally real if and only if P (a, b) is positive. The above separates
the extensions into three classes: totally real Galois, totally real non-Galois or not totally real.
   For each of these cases the quotient ζζEQ (s)
                                             (s)
                                                 is described in a different way. For the totally real Galois, the
above quotient is
                                              ζE (s)
                                                     = L(s, χ)L(s, χ),
                                              ζQ (s)
where χ is the cubic Hecke character of the extension E/Q. For the non Galois cases, we have that
                                                ζE (s)
                                                       = L(s, π),
                                                ζQ (s)
for some cusp form π of GL(2). Even more so, for the totally real case this L-function is the modular
L-function of a holomorphic modular form, while for the non totally real case it is the one associated to
a Maass form. The difference on the description of this quotient as an L-function is manifested in the
different way one describes the coefficients of the Dirichlet series of each of them. As we shall see below,
this Dirichlet series expansion is a fundamental object of study for us and thus it is undesirable not to treat
them uniformly.
   This does not happen for GL(2). For this case, the corresponding quotient is always L(s, χ) where χ
is the quadratic Hecke character. Thus, the Dirichlet series expansion is simply the one associated to the
corresponding Dirichlet Character and the coefficients are described in a straightforward way.
4.3. The p-adic Orbital Integrals.
4.3.1. The order associated to γ(a, b). We now begin our study of the orbital integral at the finite places.
We had previously defined Eγ . We are now constructing one such γ from an appropriate pair (a, b), thus
instead of writing γ(a, b) as the subindex we simply write (a, b). For example, the field E(a,b) is Eγ(a,b) .
Definition 7. Let (a, b) ∈ V (±). We denote the ring of integers of the extension E(a, b)/Q by O(a,b) or
OE if there is no possibility of confusion. As the polynomial X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk has integer coefficients,
it generates a cubic monogenic order (via its root (X)) inside E(a, b). We denote this order by R(a, b).
Remark 8. Under the identification of γ with the root (X) in E(a, b) this order is Z[γ].
     The standard theory of orders, as for example discussed in [35], implies the following result.
Lemma 1. In the context of the above discussion, Pol(a, b) is the discriminant of R(a, b) and there exists
a positive integer sγ such that
                                         Pol(a, b) = s2γ DE/Q .
Proof. This follows from the standard calculation using basis, we omit the details.                             □
                                                                                                           27

Example 2. In our previous Example 1 we considered the point (−2, 7). We have
                                     Pol(−2, 7, 5) = −2951 = −13 · 227.
Because this discriminant is square free, the root of this polynomial generates the ring of integers. Indeed,
from
                                      Pol(−2, 7, 5) = s2(−2,7) DE(−2,7) ,
we must have s(−2,7) = 1. We thus have
                                               R(−2, 7) = Z[γ],
where γ satisfies the equation X 3 + 2X 2 + 7X − 5 = 0. Notice the field generated by this extension is
not Galois. Thus there is a slight ambiguity when we choose γ. However, this doesn’t affect any of our
computations and definitions.                                                                        □
Example 3. Consider the parameters p = 5, k = 1 and the sign −. The analogous figure to Figure 1,
together with V (−) shown as the four black lines, is shown in Figure 2. The marked orange points are the

   The circle orange dots correspond to points whose root generate the ring of integers. The square orange

                                             X 3 + X 2 + 3X + 5.
This one has s(−1,3) = 2 (In [9] this is denoted by f ).                                                   □

Proposition 14. Let q be a prime number that does not divide sγ , then
                                                  O(γ, fq ) = 1.
Consequently, the product of finite orbital integrals is the finite product
                                                 Y
                                                     O(γ, fq )
                                                     q|sγ

Proof. Fix a prime q. Recall that in [48, Theorem 2.5], we have a polynomial P (t) ∈ 1 + tZ[t] such that
                                               O(γ, fq ) = P (1).
It follows from [48, Theorem 2.5 (1)] that the degree of P (t) is 2vq (sγ ). In particular, if q ∤ sγ , we have
P (t) = 1, which proves the proposition.                                                                     □

                                                                                                            29

Definition 11. Given a finite flat algebra R over Zq , we define its Cohen-Macaulay type at a maximal
ideal p:
                                     Typep (R) = dimR/p R∨ /pR∨ .
and its Cohen-Macaulay type
                                        Type(R) = max{Typep (R)}.
                                                      p

  The key fact for us
Proposition 15. We have
   (1) We have R is Gorenstein if and only if Type(R) = 1.
   (2) If Type(R) = 2, then for any fractional ideal I such that EndR (I) = R we have I ∼
                                                                                        = R or I ∼
                                                                                                 = R∨ .
Proof. Part (1) follows from [39, Proposition 3.4] and part (2) follows from [39, Theorem 3].               □
Proposition 16. Let R be a cubic ring over Zq , i.e. a finite flat Zq -algebra of rank 3. Then for every
maximal ideal m ⊂ R, the local ring Rm is a one-dimensional Cohen–Macaulay local ring of type at most
2. Consequently, R has Cohen–Macaulay type at most 2.
Proof. Since R is semi-local, it is enough to prove that type(Rm ) ≤ 2 for all maximal ideals m ⊂ R. So fix
m, and set
                                 A := Rm ,     k := A/mA,      B := A/pA.
   Because R is free over Zq , the localization A is torsion-free over Zq . Hence, multiplication by q on A is
injective. In addition, A is finite over Zq , so A is integral over Zq . Therefore
                                            dim A = dim Zq = 1.
Thus, q is a non-zero divisor and a parameter of A, so
                                           depthA ≥ 1 = dim A.
Hence A is Cohen–Macaulay (cf. [32, Proposition (I6.4.6) and Definition (I6.5.1)]).
  We now identify the Cohen–Macaulay type of A. Apply HomA (k, −) to the short exact sequence
                                                 ×q
                                        0 −→ A −−→ A −→ B −→ 0.
This gives an exact sequence
                               ×q                                               ×q
             0 → HomA (k, A) −−→ HomA (k, A) → HomA (k, B) → Ext1A (k, A) −−→ Ext1A (k, A).
Now HomA (k, A) = 0: if f : k → A is A-linear and x = f (1), then mA · x = 0. In particular qx = 0, and
since q is a non-zero divisor on A, we get x = 0. So, the exact sequence simplifies to
                                                                 ×q
                            0 −→ HomA (k, B) −→ Ext1A (k, A) −−→ Ext1A (k, A).
But Ext1A (k, A) is naturally a k-vector space and is annihilated by mA, and in particular by q. Therefore,
the last map is zero, and we obtain an isomorphism
                                         Ext1 (k, A) ∼
                                            A        = HomA (k, B).
Since HomA (k, B) = 0 :B mA = Soc(B), it follows that
                                type(A) = dimk Ext1A (k, A) = dimk Soc(B).
  So it remains to bound the k-dimension of the socle of B.
30                                   TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

  Because R is free of rank 3 over Zq , the quotient R/qR is a 3-dimensional Fq -vector space. In particular,
R/qR is Artinian. Let m be the image of m in R/qR. Then
                                            B = A/qA ∼
                                                     = (R/qR)m .
For an Artinian ring, localization at a maximal ideal is a direct factor, so (R/qR)m is an Fq -vector-space
direct factor of R/qR. Hence
                                             dimFq B ≤ 3.
On the other hand, B is an Artinian local ring with residue field k, so every composition factor of the
A-module B is isomorphic to k. Therefore
                                           dimFq B = [k : Fq ] · ℓA (B),
where ℓA (B) denotes the length of B as A-module. Thus
                                                   ℓA (B) ≤ 3.
     Let n := mA/qA be the maximal ideal of B. If B = k, then
                                               dimk Soc(B) = 1.
Otherwise n ̸= 0, and then
                                             Soc(B) = 0 :B n ⊆ n.
Indeed, if u ∈ Soc(B) \ n, then u is a unit of B, and un = 0 would force n = 0, a contradiction. Hence
B/ Soc(B) ̸= 0, so
                                      ℓA (Soc(B)) ≤ ℓA (B) − 1 ≤ 2.
Since Soc(B) is a k-vector space, its length equals its k-dimension:
                                       dimk Soc(B) = ℓA (Soc(B)) ≤ 2.
Therefore
                                         type(A) = dimk Soc(B) ≤ 2.
   Since this holds for every maximal ideal m ⊂ R, every local ring Rm has Cohen–Macaulay type at most
2. Equivalently, the semilocal ring R has Cohen–Macaulay type at most 2.                            □

     We can expand hR as a finite Dirichlet Series. We have the following result.

Then aR (·) is a multiplicative arithmetic function and
                                                    X
                                           hR (s) =    aR (d)d1−s .
                                                      d|sR

Proof. This follows from the definition.                                                                    □

Example 4. In Example 3 we see that X 3 + X 2 + 3X + 5 has s(−1,3) = 2. In particular, the only place
where the corresponding order is nonmaximal is q = 2. Consequently, if R = R(−1, 3), then
                                                          ζR2 (s)

One can verify that
                                         R2 /2R2 ≡ F2 [x]/(x + 1)3 .
We see that the right hand side has a unique maximal ideal corresponding to (x + 1). Thus, R has a unique
maximal ideal itself, and it has a residue field of cardinality 2. From this we conclude that LR2 (s) = 1.
We see that contrary to what one might expect, even though the order is non-maximal at some prime, the
corresponding factor in LR (s) can be trivial.
   The maximal order corresponding to this point is denoted O(−1,3) . It is not a monogenic order and it is
given by
                                        O(−1,3) = Z[γ, (γ + γ 2 )/2].
The indexing set R0 ⊂ R ⊂ O for R0 = R(−1, 3) consists of R(−1, 3) and O(−1,3) . For the maximal order
we have, by definition, that
                                         hO(−1,3) (s) = 1,
and
                                           [O(−1,3) : R(−1, 3)] = 2.
On the other hand, for R(−1, 3) we know that s(−1,3) = 2. Since at 2 it is Gorenstein, we have aR(−1,3) (2) =
0. Thus
                         hR(−1,3) (s) = aR(−1,3) (1) + aR(−1,3) (2)21−s = 1 + 0 = 1.
We also know [R(−1, 3) : R(−1, 3)] = 1. In conclusion, we get
                             L(s, R(−1, 3)) = LO(−1,3) (s) · 21−2s + LR(−1,3) (s).
The two involved L-functions only differ at their Euler factors at 2.                                      □

4.3.4. The conjecture and the functional equation. The conjecture that will allow us to deduce the func-
tional equation is the following.
Conjecture 1. Let R be a Gorenstein order (finite rank algebra) over Z. Let R∨ = Hom(R, Z) be its dual.
Let
                                                X
                                     JR (s) =        [R∨ : M ]−s
                                                     M ⊆R∨

be Yun’s zeta function [48]. Then we have
                                            JR (s) = L(s, R)ζQ (s).
Remark 9. The above conjecture should be stated only for GL(3). For GL(n) with n > 3, we expect a
modified formulation, which we leave to future work. In the case of GL(3), the conjecture should be provable
by applying the classification of orders established in the appendix, but we do not pursue the proof here.
Geometrically, this is related to a conjecture by I. Cherednik [16].
Remark 10. A counterexample to the above conjecture for G = GL4 : consider E = Qq [x]/(x4 − q) and γ
with characteristic polynomial
                                        f (x) = x4 − q 5 .
Note that in this case, Yun’s zeta function is easily computed, given the conjectural relation to the mo-
tivic superpolynomials as described in [16] (via describing the corresponding affine pavings of the punctual
compactified Jacobian for the curve singularity defined by f (x) or its complex analogue).
Proposition 19. In the context of the above discussion Λ(s, R) is an entire function and satisfies
                                            Λ(s, R) = Λ(1 − s, R).

element γ(a, b) for some (a, b) ∈ V (±). The pair (r1 , r2 ) is completely determined by the type of extension
in which this order sits, which in turn simply depends on whether the field Eγ is totally real or has both
real and complex embeddings. As we know, this dichotomy corresponds to Pol(a, b) > 0 or Pol(a, b) < 0.

Remark 13. Remember that our goal is to perform Poisson Summation. In order to do so, we will need
to define functions to which the summation can be applied. These requires to know the extended values
at points that are not integers, maybe not even rational, but that are in our space where the Poisson
Summation will take place. This definition allow us to do it consistently. Furthermore, on each “side” of
the singular set the choice is the same.
Remark 14. We bring the attention of the reader to the fact that there are two functions called H(x,y) .
One is a function of a complex variable, while the other is a function of both real and complex variables.
To avoid confusion, we shall not suppress the arguments of these functions.

     6. The manipulation of the regular elliptic part: Archimedean Orbital Integrals
  The purpose of this section is to see how the approximate functional equation transforms our archimedean
orbital integrals into smooth functions.

6.1. Substitution and manipulation. Up to this point the regular elliptic part of the trace formula is
                             X X
                                         vol(γ(a, b))O(γ(a, b), f ),
                                       ± (a,b)∈V (±)

                                            |Pol(a, b)|1/2 · O(γ(a, b), f∞ )
into the orbital integral in coefficient space we introduced in Definition 6. However, we must be careful
because here we are using the characteristic polynomial
                                                X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk ,
while in coefficient space we imposed the constant term to be ∓1.
  Notice that
                           Z                           Z

That is, the orbital integral is also invariant under Z+ and thus we can take instead the representative
whose determinant is ∓1. For this representative, the characteristic polynomial is
                                                     a               b

                                          Pol(za, z 2 b, z 3 c) = z 6 Pol(a, b, c).
In particular,
                           Pol(a, b) = Pol(a, b, ∓pk ) = p2k Pol(ap−k/3 , bp−2k/3 , ∓1).
Putting all together, and recalling the function θ± from Definition 6, we get
|Pol(a, b)|1/2 O(γ(a, b), f∞ ) = pk |Pol(ap−k/3 , bp−2k/3 , ∓1)|1/2 O(p−k/3 γ(a, b), f∞ ) = pk θ± (ap−k/3 , bp−2k/3 ).

Notice that the factor pk cancelled.
