Proof. This follows by picking in Theorem 1 the value A = |Pol(a, b)|α and noticing that DRγ = Pol(a, b)
because the discriminant of the order generated by γ is precisely the ideal generated by the discriminant
of its characteristic polynomial.                                                                      □
  The following result settles one of the hypothesis for performing Poisson Summation: the involved
function must be smooth and compactly supported
Theorem 2. Let ± be a sign, f and N be positive integers and fix a real number 0 < α < 1. The extension
by zero to R2 of the functions Φ±        ±
                                N,f and ΨN,f are smooth and compactly supported.
44                                 TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

Proof. Luckily for us this has already been settled in [31]. However, we wish to emphasize the fact that
the extension to the singular set must be by zero. We sketch the argument for Φ±N,f .
  Fix M ≥ 0 and let z = (x0 , y0 ) ∈ R2 be a singular element. This means Pol(x0 , y0 , ±pk ) = 0. The
continuity of Pol guarantees there exists a neighborhood around z such that
                                                 Nf2
                                                           > M.
                                              |Pol(x, y)|α
Lemma 3 implies                                             !
                                                Nf2
                                        F                       << e−M .
                                             |Pol(x, y)|α
Furthermore, θ± is the normalized orbital integral. Thus, around a central point it is bounded (the bound
of course might depend on z). We conclude that around z,
                                             Φ±
                                              N,f (x, y) << e
                                                              −M
                                                                 .
Letting M −→ ∞ we conclude the limit at z exists and its zero. This proves that the extension by zero is
indeed continuous. Smoothness follows a more convoluted argument keeping track of partial derivatives of
increasing orders.                                                                                    □
Remark 16. A reader familiar with [2] or [24] will realize that in those papers the corresponding functions
θ± are defined by invoking the germ expansions around central elements. This is an innocent abuse of
notation as long as one realizes why. Germ expansions exist around any central element but are not
uniform. In particular, one cannot really define via the germ expansions the normalized orbital integral
beyond their domain of definition. That is, very deep in the regular set a given germ expansion might not
hold. However, in the regular set one does not need the germ expansion for anything because there the
normalized orbital integral is smooth. The germ expansion are only really required to prove the smoothness
of the corresponding functions Φ±           ±
                                  N,f and ΨN,f at the central elements. This explains why we have not
introduced germ expansions at all. Their use is concentrated in the proof of the above theorem which we
refer the reader to [31] to see.

                                         7. Poisson Summation
   Our goal in this section is to perform Poisson Summation and reorganize the main term of its spectral
side. To do so, we must find the appropriate complete lattice on which the sums on the Poisson Summation
run. In order not to break the flow of the argument, we explain first the results and then prove them.
7.1. The problem of completion. The regular elliptic part of the trace formula currently is
                                          ∞
   X X X X                                X WR (n)  ±                                                   
                        aR (d)[R : Rγ ]−1            Φnd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b) + nd[R : Rγ ]2 Ψ±
                                                                                        nd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b)
    ±
                                              n
      (a,b)∈V (±) d|sγ Rγ ⊂R⊂OE                n=1

We wish to perform Poisson Summation on the pairs (a, b). However, the conditions that define V (±) rule
out several pairs of integers that would be required for this summation to be possible. As a consequence,
we must add the missing terms.
  For an (a, b) ∈ V (±) the summand associated to it is
                                          ∞
         X X                              X WR (n)  ±                                                  
                        aR (d)[R : Rγ ]−1           Φnd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b) + nd[R : Rγ ]2 Ψ±
                                                                                       nd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b)  ,
                                              n
         d|sγ Rγ ⊂R⊂OE                 n=1
                                                                                                                 45

where every dependence on γ is computed via the order Rγ = R(a, b). A brief observation of the above
sum shows that our task is to justify we can define for each missing (a, b) the following objects.
   (1) The order R(a, b).
   (2) The integer s(a,b) .
   (3) The maximal order OE .
   (4) For each cubic order R(a, b) ⊂ R ⊂ OE and d|s(a,b) define aR (d).
   (5) For each cubic order R(a, b) ⊂ R ⊂ OE and integer n define WR (n).
We see that once we know what the above objects are, the sum
                                   Φ±                       :    2 ±
                                    nd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b) + nd[R Rγ ] Ψnd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b)
is well defined because in Definition 15 and Theorem 2 we have defined them for all choices of parameters.
   Fortunately, all of these terms can be described immediately once we make the correct definition of the
order R(a, b). We recall that there is a bijection between cubic rings and binary cubic forms. We now
explain how this bijection constructs our missing term.
   Let (a, b) ∈ V (±). By definition, this means that X 3 − aX 2 + bX ∓ pk is irreducible. The order R(a, b)
is the one generated by this polynomial by adjoining its root to Z. In the same way, the binary cubic form
associated to this order is
                                        X 3 − aX 2 Y + bXY 2 ∓ pk Y 3 .
The condition (a, b) ∈ V (±) translates to this binary cubic form being irreducible. Hence, the associated
order is contained in a maximal order associated to a cubic number field (i.e. the field generated by the
same irreducible polynomial).
Definition 16. For every (a, b) ∈ Z2 . We define the order R(a, b) to be the one associated to the binary
cubic form
                                      X 3 − aX 2 Y + bXY 2 ∓ pk Y 3 .
We further define O(a,b) as the maximal order containing R(a, b) and s(a,b) = [O(a,b) : R(a, b)].
  Furthermore, the definitions and results of Subsection 4.3.2 are defined for general cubic order. Thus,
aR (d) and WR (n) are also defined. As a conclusion, we get that
                                                      ∞
         X X X X                                      X WR (n)  ±                                                  
Σ(f ) :=                           aR (d)[R : Rγ ] −1

                                                                                                   nd,[R:Rγ ] (a, b)  ,
          ±

is well defined.

□ because in our case the discriminant being a square is not the appropriate condition to be regular elliptic.

Proposition 25. Fix (a, b) and R(a, b) ⊂ R ⊂ OE . If d does not divide s(a,b) , then aR (d) = 0.
Proof. If d does not divide s(a, b), then for some prime q,
                                                      valq (d) > valq (s(a, b)).
If valq (d) > 1, then d is not square free and by definition aR (d) = 0. The remaining possibility is that
valq (d) = 1 and valq (s(a, b)) = 0. The latter equality implies that R(a, b)q is the maximal order, and
thus Gorenstein. Since R(a, b) ⊂ R ⊂ OE this in turn implies that Rq is also Gorenstein. By definition,
aR (d) = 0.                                                                                             □
  This proposition allows us to change the indexing d|sγ to simply the sum over all d, as the difference

7.3. Periodicity in the case of GL(2, Q). Theorem 3 shows that we have periodicity modulo nf 2 . We
could do all the previous analysis for the case of GL(2, Q) using the corresponding function L(s, R) instead
of Zagier’s zeta function. The difference resides on how we handle the coefficients. In the former perspective
we call them WR (n) and study them through commutative algebra. In the latter perspective, we explicitly
have the Kronecker Symbol and the congruence conditions. 
                                                               a
   Depending on the value of m, the Kronecker symbol m            has periodicity m or 4m. This leads the
                                                   2
analysis carried out in [2] to use periodicity 4nf . However, as we said above, our results imply that the
periodicity nf 2 should have been enough. In this section we will verify this is indeed the case using the
method of Altuğ. That is, we shall prove that in the computations as carried out in [2] one can break up
the trace lattice in classes modulo nf 2 , instead of 4nf 2 . With this we see our approach and Altuğ’s are
indeed consistent.
   To make the comparison easier, in this section we shall follow the notation of [2]. Thus our n is denoted
by l, the pair (a, b) is denoted by m and it is a single integer parametrizing the trace in the characteristic
polynomial

Lemma 5. The congruence conditions are periodic modulo f 2 , that is, m1 ≡ m2 (mod f 2 ) implies
                                                 1f (m1 ) = 1f (m2 ).
Proof. We have
                                  (m + f 2 )2 ∓ 4pk   m2 ∓ 4pk

                                         f2               f2
This shows that the divisibility is periodic modulo f 2 .
  We now verify the congruence. If f is odd then f has an inverse modulo 4 and thus
                           m2 ∓ 4pk

                              f2
The last congruence because f 2 ≡ 1 (mod 4). We conclude that when f is odd the class modulo 4 is that
of m2 which, being a square, is 0 or 1. This proves the invariance in this case.
   If f is even, then to have the divisibility hold we must have 2|m. Say m = 2m0 . Using that in this case

This proves the periodicity of the congruence.                                                             □
Remark 18. Notice that for those classes that satisfy the periodicity condition, we have also proven that

8.1. Definition and main properties. Based on our last manipulation, we define the following sums.
Definition 18. Let n, f be fixed integers and ±a fixed sign. We define the Kloosterman-Type sum as
                                          X          X       X
(26)                    K ± (n, f ) =                          daR (d)WR (n/d)
                                        (a,b)   mod nf 2 [R:R(a,b)]=f d|n

     Using this definition, the main part becomes
                                    ∞ X
                                      ∞
                                        K ± (n, f )
                                   XX               Z

                                    ± n=1 f =1

The function Θ±
              n,f   includes in its definition the functions Φ±        ±
                                                              n,f and Ψn,f . These in turn, are defined by
certain complex integrations over vertical lines ℜ(z) = σ. Our desire is to bring those complex integrals
outside all of the above sums and perform calculus of residues. This requires that we introduce function
of a complex variables which return as special values the sums over n and f we have above.
Definition 19. Fix a sign ±. We define the double Dirichlet series
                                                      ∞ X
                                                        ∞
                                                      X   K ± (n, f )
                                         D± (z) =                              .

                                                      n=1 f =1

Remark 19. We emphasize that in both K ± (n, f ) and D± (z) the sign ± is used to determine the binary
cubic form that defines our cubic order. Indeed, it is X 3 − aX 2 Y + bXY 2 ∓ pk Y 3 . This also depends on
our fixed prime p, which we are not making explicit in our notation. It should also notice that
                                                K − (n, f ) = K + (n, f )
which explains the reason we sometimes only write K(n, f ).
                                                                                                            53

  The following is the main result of this section and one of the fundamental results of the present paper,
whose proof will be put in the appendix.
Theorem 4. For a complex parameter z with ℜ(z) > 1, we have
                                     ζ(2z)     ζ(3z)     1 − p−z(k+1) 1 − p−z(k+2)
                         D± (z) =           ·          ·             ·
                                    ζ(z + 1) ζ(2z + 1)     1 − p−z      1 − p−2z
In particular, D± (z) is independent of the sign ± and admits a meromorphic continuation to the whole
complex plane.
8.2. Local constancy of Kloosterman sums. This subsection will be devoted to the proof of the
Theorem 3.
Lemma 6. Let R0 be an order in an étale Q-algebra and let f ≥ 1. For an overorder R ⊇ R0 with

Proof. If R ⊇ R0 and [R : R0 ] = f then f R ⊆ R0 (since R/R0 has exponent dividing f ), so I = f R is an

divisor (apply for example [41, Theorem 9.60])

   Conversely, given I with f R0 ⊆ I ⊆ R0 and I 2 ⊆ f I, define R := {x ∈ (1/f )R0 : f x ∈ I}. Then R is a

  Set R0 := R(a, b) and N := ndf 2 . Let (a′ , b′ ) ≡ (a, b) (mod N ) and write R0′ := R(a′ , b′ ).

   By Lemma 6, the set of index f overorders of R0 is in bijection with the set of R0 -submodules I satisfying

  Fix a prime q and put

(29)                                        Rq /q t Rq ∼

  A priori, the bijection (28) does not respect the commutative ring structure on both sides. The following
lemma takes care of the ring structures.
Lemma 7. Suppose we have an isomorphism of rings
                                                             ∼ ′            ′

induced overorders satisfy a canonical ring isomorphism
                                               Rq /q t Rq ∼ = R′ /q t R′ .

Proof. Transport Rq /q t Rq to Iq /q t Iq via (29) by
                                                   uv

which makes sense because Iq2 ⊆ q r Iq . To determine (u ⋆ v) mod q t Iq , it suffices to know

                                                q t+r Iq ⊇ q t+2r R0,q .

modulo q t+2r together with the image of Iq modulo q t+r Iq .
   Since ψ identifies multiplication in R0,q modulo q t+2r and identifies the subquotients Iq /q t+r Iq and

Proof of Theorem 3. We prove that the corresponding sums for (a, b) and (a′ , b′ ) coincide.
   Note that the coefficient aR (d) and WR (n) depends on the ring structure on Rq /q t Rq . Now apply
Lemma 7 to each prime q as follows. Because vq (N ) = t + 2r, the base identification (27) provides ψ at
level q t+2r . Moreover, the bijection (28) between overorders is constructed by matching the corresponding
lattices I modulo f 2 . In particular, locally at q it matches Iq and Iq′ . Thus for R′ := Φ(R) we obtain
(30)                R ⊗ Zq / q t ∼
                                 = R ′ ⊗ Zq / q t        for every prime q, where t = vq (nd).
     We now explain that (30) implies
(31)                                 WR (n) = WR′ (n),           aR (d) = aR′ (d).
(i) The coefficient WR (n). The Dirichlet coefficients WR (·) come from the Euler product for LR (s), hence
factor multiplicatively over primes. For each q, the local coefficient of q e is determined by the local Dirichlet
series depending only on the ring Rq /qRq by (11). Consequently, WR (n) is determined by the collection
of finite rings
                                           Rq /q vq (n) Rq   (q | n).
Since vq (n) ≤ vq (nd) = t, the isomorphisms (30) imply that these finite rings agree for R and R′ , hence
WR (n) = WR′ (n).
                                                                                                                  55

(ii) The coefficient aR (d). By definition aR (·) also factors over primes, and for each q the local factor
depends only on the local order Rq . In the situation at hand, the relevant local datum for p | d is whether
Rq is Gorenstein. For a finite flat Zq -algebra A, by Lemma 47.25.5 of [45, Tag 0E9M]
                    A is Gorenstein        ⇐⇒      A∨ := HomZq (A, Zq ) is a cyclic A-module,
and by Nakayama this is equivalent to A∨ /qA∨ being cyclic over A/qA. Note that
                A∨ /qA∨ ∼                   = HomZ (A, Zq /qZq ) ∼
                          = HomZ (A, Zq )/q ∼
                                       q                         q= Hom(A/qA, Fq ).
Thus the Gorenstein property for A = Rq is determined by the ring Rq /qRq .
  Since q | d implies t = vq (nd) ≥ 1, (30) yields Rq /qRq ∼
                                                           = Rq′ /qRq′ , hence Rq is Gorenstein iff Rq′ is
Gorenstein. Therefore aR (d) = aR′ (d).
  Combining (i) and (ii) gives (31).
  Finally, the bijection Φ in (28) is a bijection between the index-s overorders of R0 and those of R0′ , and by
(31) it preserves each summand aR (d)WR (n). Hence the two sums coincide, proving the proposition. □

8.4. Parametrization of cubic rings. For a given (a, b) (mod q v+2r ) the local Kloosterman sums have
a middle sum whose indexing set is
                                       R ⊇ R(a, b),           [R : R(a, b)] = q r .
The main task in the explicit computation of the value of these sums corresponds to a concrete construction
of the above set from which the values
                                      aR (q t ),     WR (q v−t ),            0 ≤ t ≤ v,
can be explicitly computed. In this section we will explain this concrete construction. We will see that
it reduces to finding all solutions to a particular system of equations. In the appendices we will solve the
system for each choice of values p and q.

8.4.1. Delone - Faddeev Correspondence. Given g ∈ GL(2, Q) and a binary cubic form
                                       f (x, y) = ax3 + bx2 y + cxy 2 + dy 3 ,
we consider the action
                                            f g (x, y) = det(g)f ((x, y)g −1 ).
One checks that the discriminant
                             Disc(f ) := b2 c2 − 4ac3 − 4b3 d − 27a2 d2 + 18abcd ∈ Z
satisfies Disc(f g ) = det(g)−2 Disc(f ).
Proposition 28. [26, Proposition 4.2] There is a bijection between the set of GL(2, Z)-orbits on the space
of binary cubic forms with integer coefficients and the set of isomorphism classes of cubic rings A.
  This is called Delone–Faddeev correspondence, which was first studied in their book [20]. We recall the
construction of the bijection. Let A = Z + Zα + Zβ be a cubic ring. We assume that {1, α, β} is a good
basis (cf. [20]) in the sense that αβ = n ∈ Z. Note that good basis always exists. In this case we have
                                           
                                           αβ = n,
                                           
                                           
                                             α2 = m + bα − aβ,
                                           β 2 = ℓ + dα − cβ,
                                           
                                           
58                                  TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

such that a, b, c, d, m, n, ℓ are integers satisfying
                                                    
                                                    n = −ad
                                                    
                                                    
                                                      m = −ac
                                                    
                                                    ℓ = −bd
                                                    

We associate to A the binary cubic form
                                     f (x, y) = ax3 + bx2 y + cxy 2 + dy 3 .
Remark 21. As is remarked in [26, Page 10], Deligne observed that the above parametrization is functorial
in the base scheme. Hence we can replace Z by Zp and still obtain a bijection.
   To evaluate aR (q t ) we must have a criteria to determine whether a given overorder is Gorenstein. The
following result is such criterion.
Definition 20. We call f (x, y) = ax3 + bx2 y + cxy 2 + dy 3 primitive if we have gcd(a, b, c, d) = 1.
Proposition 29. [26, Proposition 5.2] The form f (x, y) = ax3 + bx2 y + cxy 2 + dy 3 is primitive if and only
if the associated ring is Gorenstein.
8.4.2. Parametrization of overorders.
Proposition 30. Let K = GL(2, Zq ) and let f be a binary cubic form over Zq with associated cubic

Proof. Let (1, ω, θ) be a good basis of A and
                                      L := A/Zq ,         LQq := L ⊗Zq Qq .
By the functoriality of Delone–Faddeev correspondence (see the remark in [26, Page 10]), A determines an

whose coordinate polynomial in the basis                  

  For g ∈ Dq,r , let Lg ⊂ LQq be the lattice with basis
                                                     eg := g −1 e.
                                                                                                            59

Since g ∈ Mat2×2 (Zq ), we have L ⊂ Lg , and because e = geg ,

                                           [Lg : L] = q vq (det g) = q r .
If (x, y) are the row coordinates in the basis eg , then

                                             (x, y)eg = (x, y)g −1 e,
while
                                           ω̄ ∧ θ̄ = det(g) (ω̄g ∧ θ̄g ).
Hence the coordinate polynomial of ΦA in the basis eg is

                                        det(g) f ((x, y)g −1 ) = f g (x, y).
Because the old basis and the new basis satisfy e = geg , the transformed form is f g , and changing the
basis eg by k ∈ K replaces g by gk.
  Therefore
                                                                   ^2
                              f g ∈ VZq ⇐⇒ ΦA ∈ Sym3 (L∨      g)⊗     Lg .
When this happens, the same functorial correspondence gives a cubic Zq -order with quotient lattice Lg
and attached form f g . Over Qq it is still the same tensor ΦA , so its generic fiber is E. Thus R(f g ) is an
overorder of A inside E, and
                                        [R(f g ) : A] = [Lg : L] = q r .
So Ψ is well defined on Sr (f ).
  Conversely, let R ⊃ A be a cubic Zq -order with [R : A] = q r , and put
                                                  LR := R/Zq .
Then L ⊂ LR ⊂ LQq and [LR : L] = q r . Choose a basis eR of LR . Since L ⊂ LR , there exists g ∈
Mat2×2 (Zq ) such that
                                                     e = geR .
Necessarily vq (det g) = r, so g ∈ χq,r . Repeating the above change-of-basis computation, the binary cubic
form attached to R in the basis eR is f g . Hence f g ∈ VZq , so gK ∈ Sr (f ), and
                                                  Ψ(gK) = R.
If we replace eR by another basis k −1 eR with k ∈ K, then g is replaced by gk. Thus the coset gK depends
only on R. This proves surjectivity.
   Finally, if Ψ(g1 K) = Ψ(g2 K), then the corresponding quotient lattices Lg1 and Lg2 are equal. Hence
their chosen bases differ by an element of K:
                                          eg2 = k −1 eg1         (k ∈ K).
Equivalently,
                                                g2−1 e = k −1 g1−1 e,
so g2 = g1 k, i.e. g1 K = g2 K. Thus Ψ is injective, and therefore bijective.                               □

Remark 23. The case r = 0 also contributes to the Kloosterman sums. However, in that case the only
indexing order is R(a, b) itself and the computation can be treated directly.
Remark 24. From the above discussion we can see how different choices will affect the analysis of solutions.
For example, from system (36) one can see that u is a unit when p ̸= q. Similarly, from the proof of
Proposition 33 we see that if q ̸= 3, we have val(u) ≥ 2β which reduces the cases one has to consider.
   The central problem to evaluate the Kloosterman sums is to determine the solutions of the system
from Theorem (5). This is the content of the appendices. As can be seen there, the construction of the
corresponding cubic form via the above systems will also tell us the value of aR (d) and WR (n).

                          9. The Analysis of Residues and the Isolation of the Traces
     Recall that by definition

Lemma 9. Assume (p, 6q) = 1. Fix integers r ≥ 1 and β ≥ 0 with 3β < r, and consider the system (43)
with unknowns
                            u ∈ (Z/p r−2β Z)× ,   (a, b) ∈ (Z/p 2r Z)2 .

Proof. We treat the cases β = 0 and β ≥ 1 separately.
Case 1: β = 0. Then the first congruence in (43) is vacuous. Choose u ∈ (Z/pr Z)× arbitrarily, which
gives (p − 1)pr−1 choices. Fix such a u and choose a ∈ Z/p2r Z arbitrarily for the moment.
   The second congruence in (43) determines b modulo pr uniquely:
                                         b ≡ −3u2 − 2au              (mod pr ),
so we may write
                            b = b0 + pr B,        b0 := −3u2 − 2au,                 B ∈ Z/pr Z.
Substitute this into the third congruence in (43) (whose modulus is now p2r ):
                               u3 + au2 + bu + q = u3 + au2 + (b0 + pr B)u + q
                                                         = u3 + au2 + b0 u + q +pr Bu.
                                                           |       {z        }
                                                              =−2u3 −au2 +q

Thus the third congruence is equivalent to
(44)                                  pr Bu ≡ 2u3 + au2 − q             (mod p2r ).
Since u is a unit, (44) has a solution B if and only if the right-hand side is divisible by pr , i.e.
(45)                                    2u3 + au2 − q ≡ 0             (mod pr ).
                                                                                                         69

For fixed u, congruence (45) determines a modulo pr uniquely, hence among a mod p2r it leaves exactly pr
possibilities. Once a satisfies (45), the congruence (44) determines B mod pr uniquely (again because u is
a unit), hence determines b uniquely modulo p2r .
  Therefore, for each u there are exactly pr choices of a and then a unique b, so
                                  Nr,0 = (p − 1)pr−1 · pr = (p − 1)p2r−1 .

Case 2: β ≥ 1. Write
                                    a = −3u + pβ A,       A ∈ Z/p2r−β Z,
so that the first congruence in (43) is automatically satisfied. Then the second congruence becomes
          3u2 + 2(−3u + pβ A)u + b ≡ 0     (mod pr−β )     ⇐⇒       b ≡ 3u2 − 2pβ Au   (mod pr−β ).
Hence we may write
                              b = 3u2 − 2pβ Au + pr−β B,        B ∈ Z/pr+β Z,
since b is taken modulo p2r and 2r − (r − β) = r + β.
   Now substitute these expressions for a and b into the third congruence in (43). A direct cancellation
gives
                 u3 + au2 + bu + q = u3 + (−3u + pβ A)u2 + (3u2 − 2pβ Au + pr−β B)u + q
                                   = u3 − pβ Au2 + pr−β Bu + q.
Thus the third congruence (modulo p2r−3β ) is equivalent to
(46)                          pr−β Bu ≡ −(u3 + q) + pβ Au2      (mod p2r−3β ).
Since 3β < r, we have r − β ≥ 1, so the left side is divisible by pr−β . Therefore (46) has a solution B if
and only if the right side is divisible by pr−β , i.e.
(47)                                   u3 + q ≡ pβ Au2    (mod pr−β ).
Because u is a unit, (47) implies in particular
                                            u3 ≡ −q      (mod p).
By definition, the number of such residue classes u mod p is np (q), and since p ̸= 3 (because (p, 6q) = 1)
and u ̸≡ 0 (mod p), Hensel’s lemma [45, Tag 04GM] shows that each such root modulo p lifts uniquely to
a root modulo pβ of the equation U 3 + q = 0. Consequently, the number of u mod pr−2β satisfying u3 ≡ −q
(mod pβ ) is
                                     np (q) · p(r−2β)−β = np (q) pr−3β ,
since once u is fixed modulo pβ , there are p(r−2β)−β = pr−3β free lifts to modulo pr−2β .
   Now fix such a u. Condition (47) determines A modulo pr−2β uniquely, because dividing (47) by pβ u2
(a unit times pβ ) gives
                                              u3 + q
                                         A ≡ β 2 (mod pr−2β ).
                                               p u
Since A is taken modulo p2r−β , this leaves exactly
                                           p(2r−β)−(r−2β) = pr+β
choices for A.
70                                   TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

  Finally, once u and A satisfy (47), the congruence (46) determines B modulo p(2r−3β)−(r−β) = pr−2β
uniquely (because u is a unit), and since B is taken modulo pr+β , this leaves
                                                 p(r+β)−(r−2β) = p3β
choices for B.
  Multiplying the independent counts gives
                                Nr,β = np (q)pr−3β · pr+β · p3β = np (q) p2r+β ,
                                                  

as claimed.                                                                                                   □

(43) with β = m and unknowns

                   u3 + au2 + bu + q = u3 + (−3u + pm A)u2 + (3u2 − 2pm Au + p2m B)u + q

(51)                              p2m Bu ≡ −(u3 + q) + pm Au2           (mod p3m ).

(52)                                     u3 + q ≡ pm Au2         (mod p2m ).

choices for A.

which is well-defined because we work modulo p3m . Dividing (51) by p2m yields

Proposition 41. Assume (p, 6) = 1 and q = pk with k > 0. Setting x = p−z ,

Proof. For every a, b, the ring R(a, b) is monogenic, hence Gorenstein [45, Tag 0DWA], therefore aR(a,b) (p) =

                                                        X

  Recall that WR(a,b) (pv ) depends only on the factorization of fa,b (X, 1) mod p. Since q = pk ≡ 0 (mod p),

Proposition 42. Assume (p, 6) = 1 and q = pk with k > 0. Fix integers r ≥ 3β ≥ 0 with r > 0, and set
                                 n := r − 2β,         M := 2r − 3β,             g := gcd(3, p − 1).
Define
         I1 := {t ∈ Z : β ≤ t ≤ n − 1, 3t < k},                 I2 := {t ∈ Z : 0 ≤ t ≤ n − 1, 3t > k, 2t ≤ k − β}.

                                         u ∈ Z/pr−2β Z,               (a, b) ∈ (Z/p2r Z)2 ,
equals                                                                                                     
                                                                                   X             X
                         N (p, r, β, k) = 1k≥M p3r + (p − 1)p2r+2β−1                     pt +          pt  + Tt0 ,

then                                                                
                        β b −β+1 − 1)                                   a    b −a +1
                      p (p 1

            X
                  t                   ,       b1 ≥ β,       X
                                                                  t                    ,   b2 ≥ a2 ,
                 p =        p−1                                  p =          p−1
            t∈I1     0,
                     
                                              b1 < β,       t∈I2     0,
                                                                     
                                                                                           b2 < a2 .

Proof. We first solve the first two congruences in (93). For a fixed u, they are equivalent to
(94)                           a = −3u + pβ α,          b = 3u2 − 2upβ α + pr−β γ,
with
                                      α ∈ Z/p2r−β Z,      γ ∈ Z/pr+β Z.
Indeed, the first congruence gives the formula for a, and then the second congruence gives the formula for
b. Conversely, every choice of (α, γ) produces a pair (a, b) satisfying the first two congruences, so (94) is a
bijective parametrization.
   Substituting (94) into the third congruence in (93) gives
(95)                                pβ u2 α − pr−β uγ ≡ u3 + q     (mod pM ).
Hence the problem is to count, for each u ∈ Z/pn Z, the number of pairs (α, γ) satisfying (95).
1. The shell u = 0. If u = 0, then (95) becomes
                                                q≡0      (mod pM ).
Thus there are no solutions unless k ≥ M . If k ≥ M , then (95) is automatic, and α and γ are arbitrary.
Therefore the number of solutions with u = 0 is
                                           1k≥M p2r−β pr+β = 1k≥M p3r .

2. Solvability for a nonzero shell. Now assume u ̸= 0, and write
                             u = pt u0 ,      0 ≤ t ≤ n − 1,      u0 ∈ (Z/pn−t Z)× .
Since r − β = n + β, congruence (95) becomes
(96)                          pβ+2t u20 α − pn+β+t u0 γ ≡ p3t u30 + pk   (mod pM ).
Because t ≤ n − 1, we have n − t ≥ 1, hence
                                              n + β + t > (β + 2t).
Therefore the image of the left-hand side of (96) is precisely the ideal pβ+2t Z/pM Z. Consequently, (96) is
solvable if and only if
(97)                                          ordp (u3 + q) ≥ β + 2t.
We now analyze (97) by comparing 3t with k.
  Case 1: 3t < k. Then
                                         u3 + q = p3t (u30 + pk−3t ),
and the factor in parentheses is a unit. Hence ordp (u3 + q) = 3t, so (97) is equivalent to
                                             3t ≥ β + 2t ⇐⇒ t ≥ β.
Thus the admissible shells in this case are exactly t ∈ I1 .
  Case 2: 3t > k. Then
                                         u3 + q = pk (1 + p3t−k u30 ),
                                                                                                           85

and again the factor in parentheses is a unit. Hence ordp (u3 + q) = k, so (97) is equivalent to
                                        k ≥ β + 2t ⇐⇒ 2t ≤ k − β.
Thus the admissible shells in this case are exactly t ∈ I2 .
  Case 3: 3t = k. Then necessarily 3 | k, and t = t0 := k/3. We have
                                            u3 + q = pk (u30 + 1).
If t0 ≥ β, then (97) is automatic, so every nonzero u with ordp (u) = t0 is admissible. If t0 < β, then (97)
is equivalent to
                                           ordp (u30 + 1) ≥ β − t0 ,
that is,
(98)                                       u30 ≡ −1    (mod pβ−t0 ).
The polynomial X 3 + 1 has exactly g = gcd(3, p − 1) roots in Fp , and all of them are simple because p ̸= 3.
By Hensel’s lemma [45, Tag 04GM], (98) therefore has exactly g solutions modulo pβ−t0 . Each such residue
class has
                                           p(n−t0 )−(β−t0 ) = pn−β
lifts modulo pn−t0 . Hence, when t0 < β, the number of admissible u in the shell ordp (u) = t0 is
                                                    g pn−β .
This completes the solvability analysis.
3. Number of pairs (α, γ) for a fixed admissible u. Fix an admissible nonzero u = pt u0 . Dividing
(96) by pβ+2t gives
(99)                                u20 α − pn−t u0 γ ≡ λu       (mod p2n−2t ),
for a uniquely determined residue class λu modulo p2n−2t . Since u20 is a unit, for each fixed γ ∈ Z/pr+β Z,
congruence (99) determines α uniquely modulo p2n−2t . As α ranges modulo p2r−β , this gives exactly
                                           p(2r−β)−(2n−2t) = p3β+2t
choices for α. Since there are pr+β choices for γ, the number of pairs (α, γ) corresponding to this fixed
admissible u is
(100)                                      pr+β p3β+2t = pr+4β+2t .
By the bijection (94), this is also the number of pairs (a, b) modulo p2r corresponding to this u.
4. Contribution of each shell. Suppose first that t ∈ I1 ∪ I2 . Then every nonzero u with ordp (u) = t
is admissible, and the number of such u modulo pn is
                                                 (p − 1)pn−t−1 .
Using (100), the contribution of this shell is
                               (p − 1)pn−t−1 · pr+4β+2t = (p − 1)p2r+2β+t−1 .
Summing over t ∈ I1 and t ∈ I2 yields
                                                                                
                                                          X             X
                                    (p − 1)p2r+2β−1             pt +          pt  .
                                                          t∈I1          t∈I2
86                                     TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

  Now assume 3 | k and 0 ≤ t0 ≤ n − 1, so that the shell 3t = k exists. If t0 ≥ β, then the number of u
with ordp (u) = t0 is again (p − 1)pn−t0 −1 , so its contribution is
                                 (p − 1)pn−t0 −1 · pr+4β+2t0 = (p − 1)p2r+2β+t0 −1 .
If t0 < β, then there are gpn−β admissible values of u, so the contribution is
                                          gpn−β · pr+4β+2t0 = gp2r+β+2t0 .
These are exactly the two cases in the definition of Tt0 .
  Adding the contribution of the shell u = 0 from Step 1, the contributions of the shells in I1 ∪ I2 , and
the shell t = t0 when it exists, we obtain the stated formula for N (p, r, β, k).
  Finally, the geometric-series expressions are immediate from
                           I1 = {t ∈ Z : β ≤ t ≤ b1 },        I2 = {t ∈ Z : a2 ≤ t ≤ b2 },
with the convention that these sets are empty when the lower bound exceeds the upper bound.                     □
B.3. The case when v ≥ 1 and r ≥ 1. Fix an odd prime p and q = pk (k > 0) such that (p, 6) = 1. Fix

   Consider the congruence system
                                
                                                    β

                                
                                
(101)                            3u2 + 2au + b ≡ 0 (mod pr−β ),
                                u3 + au2 + bu + q ≡ 0 (mod p2r−3β ),
                                

1. The shell u = 0. If u = 0, then (104) says q ≡ 0 (mod pM ), so there are no solutions unless k ≥ M .

   If C0 = 0, then
                                        y(Ax2 − Bxy) = xy(Ax − By).
Thus:
      • if A ̸= 0 and B ̸= 0, we get three distinct linear factors, hence type (1, 1, 1), which contributes
        (p − 1)2 ;

So Uτ (0) is as stated.
   If C0 = 1, then the type of y(Ax2 − Bxy + y 2 ) is the type of the quadratic factor Ax2 − Bxy + y 2 ,
together with the extra linear factor y. We separate the case A = 0:
      • if A = 0 and B = 0, we get y 3 , hence type (13 );
      • if A = 0 and B ̸= 0, we get y 2 (y − Bx), hence type (1, 12 ); this contributes p − 1.

        this gives A = 0, already excluded. Hence there are exactly p − 1 such pairs (A, B) with A ̸= 0.

Proof. The first two congruences in (112) are equivalent to

   If 3t < k, then u3 + pk = p3t (u30 + pk−3t ), and the factor in parentheses is a unit. Thus vp (u3 + pk ) =

This proves part (I).
3. The contribution of u ̸= 0 when k < 3β. Assume now k < 3β and 3 | k, and write t0 := k/3 < β.

(120)                              u20 x − pβ−t0 u0 y ≡ mu,B,C       (mod p2β−2t0 ),

(121)                                pv+4β−1 · pv+3β+2t0 −1 = p2v+7β+2t0 −2 .
This number is independent of (B, C). Therefore, for fixed admissible u, the pairs (B, C) ∈ F2p are
equidistributed.
4. Fixed-A factorization counts. For each A ∈ Fp , let Pτ (A) be the number of pairs (B, C) ∈ F2p such
that t3 − At2 + Bt − C has factorization type τ . If s = A/3 ∈ Fp , then the change of variables t = x + s
gives a bijection between the fiber with coefficient A and the fiber with coefficient 0, and it preserves
factorization type. Hence Pτ (A) is independent of A; denote the common value by Pτ .
   Let Tτ be the total number of monic cubics over Fp of type τ . Since there are p possible values of A, we
have
                                                Tτ = pPτ .

Then Proposition 42 and Proposition 43 show that:
     • the u = 0 shell is on the boundary when k = 2n + β, and in the tail when k ≥ 2n + β + 1;
     • if t > β, then the shell ordp (u) = t is on the boundary when k = β + 2t, and in the tail when
       k ≥ β + 2t + 1;
     • if t = β < n, then the shell is on the boundary when k = 3t, and in the tail when k ≥ 3t + 1;
     • if t < β, then the shell occurs only for k = 3t (the Hensel shell).
For r = 3β (so β ≥ 1), Proposition 44 gives the same dichotomy: the u = 0 shell is on the boundary when

Definition 23. Assume (q, 2) = 1 and p = 2. Fix integers r ≥ 3β ≥ 0 with r > 0. Let N (r, β) be the
number of solutions (u, a, b) to the system (126).
Proposition 46. With the above notation,
                                                      
                                                      2 2r−1 ,   β = 0,
                                         N (r, β) =
                                                      2 2r+β ,   β ≥ 1.
In particular, N (r, β) is independent of q as long as (q, 2) = 1.
Proof. Fix u ∈ (Z/2r−2β Z)× (so u is odd).
  From the second congruence in (126),
(127)                                 b ≡ −(3u2 + 2au)       (mod 2r−β ).
  Reduce the third congruence in (126) modulo 2r−β and substitute (127):
           u3 + au2 + bu + q ≡ u3 + au2 − u(3u2 + 2au) + q ≡ q − 2u3 − au2 ≡ 0        (mod 2r−β ).
Thus we must have
(128)                                   au2 ≡ q − 2u3       (mod 2r−β ).
Since u is odd, u2 is invertible modulo 2r−β , so (128) has a unique solution
(129)                                 a ≡ (q − 2u3 ) u−2     (mod 2r−β ).
 The first congruence in (126) is a ≡ −3u (mod 2β ). Since r ≥ 3β implies r − β ≥ β, reducing (129)
modulo 2β and comparing gives
                       (q − 2u3 )u−2 ≡ −3u     (mod 2β ) ⇐⇒ q + u3 ≡ 0       (mod 2β ).
Hence the first congruence holds if and only if
(130)                                        u3 ≡ −q      (mod 2β ).
  If β = 0, (130) is vacuous and u can be any odd residue modulo 2r , so there are 2r−1 choices.
102                                          TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

    If β ≥ 1, the cube map is a bijection on (Z/2β Z)× , hence (130) has exactly one solution u0 modulo 2β
(since −q is odd). Because u is taken modulo 2r−2β and r − 2β ≥ β (equivalently r ≥ 3β), the number of
lifts of u0 to an element of (Z/2r−2β Z)× is
                                                    #{u} = 2(r−2β)−β = 2r−3β .
     For each admissible u, the congruence (129) fixes a modulo 2r−β , hence the number of lifts a ∈ Z/22r Z
is
                                        #{a} = 2(2r)−(r−β) = 2r+β .
Given (u, a), the third congruence in (126) determines b uniquely modulo 22r−3β (since u is invertible):
                                           b ≡ −(u3 + au2 + q) u−1               (mod 22r−3β ).
Therefore the number of lifts b ∈ Z/22r Z is
                                                    #{b} = 22r−(2r−3β) = 23β .
     If β = 0, we get
                                             N (r, 0) = (2r−1 ) · (2r ) · (20 ) = 22r−1 .
If β ≥ 1, we get
                                           N (r, β) = (2r−3β ) · (2r+β ) · (23β ) = 22r+β .
                                                                                                                  □

                     Appendix D. Calculation of Local Kloosterman sum: p = 3
  We consider the case p = 3 and (3, q) = 1.
D.1. The case r = 0.
Proposition 49. Assume (q, 3) = 1 and p = 3, and set x = 3−z . Then
        ∞

                                                                           

Proof. For r = 0, the definition (136) reduces to summing WR(a,b) (3v ) over (a, b) mod 3v (since only
R = R(a, b) occurs and aR (3t ) vanishes for t ≥ 2). Thus K3 (3v , 1) = (a,b) mod 3v WR(a,b) (3v ).
                                                                          P

   The local coefficient WR(a,b) (3v ) depends only on the residue-field degrees of R(a, b)⊗Z3 (i.e. the degrees
of the distinct irreducible factors of fa,b (X) = X 3 − aX 2 + bX − q modulo 3). For p = 3 and (q, 3) = 1,
among the 9 pairs (a, b) mod 3 one checks:
                   #{(a, b) : fa,b ≡ (linear)3 } = 1,          #{(a, b) : fa,b ≡ (linear)2 (linear)} = 1,
      #{(a, b) : fa,b ≡ (linear)(irreducible quadratic)} = 3,              #{(a, b) : fa,b irreducible cubic} = 4.
(The counts are the same for q ≡ 1, 2 (mod 3).)
                                                                                                              111

  For these residue types, the ratio ζR,3 (s)/ζQ,3 (s) gives:

               W (3v ) = 0 (v ≥ 1) for type (13 ) (a triple root),           W (3v ) = 1 for type (1, 12 ),

                            W (3v ) = 12|v for type (1, 2),        W (3v ) = gv for type (3).

            v=0                     v≥1                                              v≥1

and summing the three geometric series yields (136).                                                          □

D.2. The case when v = 0 and r ≥ 1. Consider the system

Proposition 50. Assume (q, 3) = 1 and p = 3. Fix integers r ≥ 3β ≥ 0 with r > 0. Let N (r, β) be the
number of solutions (u, a, b) to (138). Then
                                                    N (r, 0) = 2 · 32r−1 ,
and for β ≥ 1 one has
                  N (r, β) = sβ (q) · 32r+β ,         sβ (q) := #{u mod 3β : u3 ≡ −q (mod 3β )}.
Moreover,
                                                          s1 (q) = 1,
and for every β ≥ 2,
                                                   (
                                                    3,       q ≡ ±1     (mod 9),
                                          sβ (q) =
                                                    0,       q ̸≡ ±1    (mod 9).
In particular, for β = 1 (hence r ≥ 3),
                                                      N (r, 1) = 32r+1 ,
and for β ≥ 2 (hence r ≥ 6),
                                               (
                                                32r+β+1 ,         q ≡ ±1 (mod 9),
                                    N (r, β) =
                                                0,                q ̸≡ ±1 (mod 9).
112                                 TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

Proof. We work with the system (138).
  From 3u + a ≡ 0 (mod 3β ), write
                                     a = −3u + 3β α,        α ∈ Z/32r−β Z.
Plugging this into 3u2 + 2au + b ≡ 0 (mod 3r−β ) gives
                                      b ≡ 3u2 − 2 · 3β αu    (mod 3r−β ),
so we can write
                              b = 3u2 − 2 · 3β αu + 3r−β γ,    γ ∈ Z/3r+β Z.
Substituting these into the third congruence yields the single congruence
(139)                          u3 − 3β αu2 + 3r−β γu + q ≡ 0     (mod 32r−3β ).
Thus counting solutions (u, a, b) is equivalent to counting triples (u, α, γ) with u mod 3r−2β , α mod 32r−β ,
γ mod 3r+β satisfying (139).
  Consider the case β = 0. Then (139) becomes
                                    u3 − αu2 + 3r γu + q ≡ 0    (mod 32r ).
Reducing mod 3 shows u must be a unit mod 3 (hence a unit mod 3r ), because if 3 | u then the left side
is ≡ q ̸≡ 0 (mod 3). So u ranges over (Z/3r Z)× , which has φ(3r ) = 2 · 3r−1 elements. For each such
unit u and each γ mod 3r , the coefficient u2 is invertible mod 32r , so the congruence uniquely determines
α mod 32r . Hence
                     N (r, 0) = #(Z/3r Z)× · #(Z/3r Z) = (2 · 3r−1 ) · 3r = 2 · 32r−1 .
   Consider the case β ≥ 1. Reducing (139) mod 3 again forces u to be a unit. Moreover, reducing (139)
mod 3β kills the γ-term (since r − β ≥ 2β from r ≥ 3β) and kills the α-term (it has an explicit factor 3β ),
so we must have
                           u3 + q ≡ 0 (mod 3β ),     i.e. u3 ≡ −q (mod 3β ).
Let sβ (q) denote the number of solutions u mod 3β to u3 ≡ −q (mod 3β ). Since the condition depends
only on u mod 3β and u is taken modulo 3r−2β with r − 2β ≥ β, the number of admissible u mod 3r−2β
equals
                                    sβ (q) · 3(r−2β)−β = sβ (q) · 3r−3β .
   Now fix such a u. Because u is a unit, (139) is a linear congruence in α modulo 32r−3β with leading
coefficient 3β u2 . Since u3 +q ≡ 0 (mod 3β ) and 3r−β γu is automatically divisible by 3β , the right-hand side
is divisible by 3β , so for each γ there is a unique solution for α modulo 3(2r−3β)−β = 32r−4β , hence exactly
33β lifts to α mod 32r−β . Independently, γ ranges over 3r+β values. Therefore, for each fixed admissible u
there are
                                                3r+β · 33β = 3r+4β
choices of (α, γ).
   Multiplying by the number of admissible u gives
                                N (r, β) = sβ (q) · 3r−3β · 3r+4β = sβ (q) · 32r+β .
                                                         

   Finally, s1 (q) = 1 since u3 ≡ u (mod 3). For β ≥ 2, a unit cube modulo 9 is always ≡ ±1 (mod 9), hence
 3
u ≡ −q (mod 9) is solvable iff −q ≡ ±1 (mod 9), in that case one checks there are exactly 3 solutions
mod 9, and these lift uniquely to all higher powers 3β (so sβ (q) = 3 for all β ≥ 2). This proves the stated
explicit values of sβ (q) and completes the proof.                                                         □

Proof. We first put every solution into a convenient normal form.
Step 1: any solution has 3 ∤ u. If 3 | u, then the first two congruences imply 3 | a and 3 | b, so the
left-hand side of the third congruence is congruent to q ̸≡ 0 (mod 3), impossible. Hence every solution has
u ∈ (Z/3r−2β Z)× .
Step 2: parameterization of the fibers. For a unit u, write
                          a = −3u + 3β (A + 3α),            b = −3u2 − 2au + 3r−β (B + 3η),
with
                                     α ∈ Z/3v+2r−β−1 Z,           η ∈ Z/3v+r+β−1 Z.
Then A ≡ (a + 3u)/3β (mod 3) and B ≡ (3u2 + 2au + b)/3r−β (mod 3), and a direct substitution gives
(144)            E := u3 + au2 + bu + q = u3 + q − 3β Au2 − 3β+1 αu2 + 3r−β Bu + 3r−β+1 ηu.
116                                 TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

Step 3: the case r > 3β. Reduce (144) modulo 3β+1 . Since r − β ≥ β + 1 when r > 3β, the B-term
vanishes modulo 3β+1 , so
                              u3 + q − 3β Au2 ≡ 0 (mod 3β+1 ).
Thus 3β | u3 + q, and for each admissible u the residue A is forced by
                                                 u3 + q
(145)                                       A ≡ u−2      (mod 3).
                                                   3β
Now fix such a unit u and fix B, C ∈ F3 . After A has been chosen by (145), the condition
                                                 E
                                              C≡       (mod 3)
                                              32r−3β
is equivalent to one linear congruence modulo 32r−4β in the variable α, namely
                             −u2 α + 3r−2β−1 uη ≡ κ(u, A, B, C)         (mod 32r−4β )
for a suitable constant κ(u, A, B, C). Since u2 is a unit, for each choice of η there are exactly
                                         3(v+2r−β−1)−(2r−4β) = 3v+3β−1
choices of α. Therefore, for fixed admissible (u, B, C), the number of lifts (a, b) is
(146)                              L> := 3v+r+β−1 · 3v+3β−1 = 32v+r+4β−2 .
So, once u is fixed, the pairs (B, C) ∈ F23 are equidistributed.
  To finish the case r > 3β, we only need two elementary counting lemmas.
Quadratic type counts. Fix A ∈ F3 and consider
                                         f (x, y) = y(Ax2 − Bxy + Cy 2 ).
If A = 0, then f = y 2 (−Bx + Cy), so among the 9 pairs (B, C) we get:
                  1 form of type 0,         2 forms of type (13 ),      6 forms of type (1, 12 ).
If A ̸= 0, multiplying the quadratic factor by A−1 does not change the splitting type, so we may take
A = 1. Then the discriminant is
                                    ∆ = B 2 − 4AC = B 2 − C ∈ F3 .
For each fixed B and each prescribed ∆ ∈ F3 , there is a unique C, so each of the values 0, 1, 2 occurs
exactly three times. Hence:
                        ∆ = 0 ⇒ (1, 12 ),        ∆ = 1 ⇒ (1, 1, 1),       ∆ = 2 ⇒ (1, 2),
with three occurrences of each type.
Subcase (I.a): β = 0. Now (145) becomes A ≡ u + q (mod 3), so among the two unit residue classes
modulo 3, exactly one gives A = 0 and exactly one gives A ̸= 0. Each such residue class has 3r−1 lifts
modulo 3r . By (146), each fixed (B, C) therefore occurs
                                 3r−1 L> = 3r−1 · 32v+r−2 = 32r+2v−3 =: m0
times in each of the two A-families. Combining this with the quadratic type counts above gives
                   N0 (v, r, 0) = m0 ,      N(13 ) (v, r, 0) = 2m0 ,    N(1,12 ) (v, r, 0) = 9m0 ,
                              N(1,1,1) (v, r, 0) = 3m0 ,      N(1,2) (v, r, 0) = 3m0 .
This is exactly Proposition 52(I.a).
                                                                                                                117

Subcase (I.b): β = 1. The admissibility condition is u3 + q ≡ 0 (mod 3), so u ≡ −q (mod 3), hence
there are 3r−3 admissible classes modulo 3r−2 . For such a class, u3 (mod 9) depends only on u (mod 3),
so the forced residue
                                                 u3 + q
                                         A ≡ u−2         (mod 3)
                                                    3
is constant on that class. Evaluating at u ≡ −q (mod 3) gives exactly the quantity A1 (q) appearing in the
statement:                                   
                                             0, q ≡ 1, 8 (mod 9),
                                             
                                             
                                     A1 (q) = 1, q ≡ 2, 4 (mod 9),
                                             
                                             2, q ≡ 5, 7 (mod 9).
                                             

By (146), each fixed pair (B, C) occurs
                                 3r−3 L> = 3r−3 · 32v+r+2 = 32r+2v−1 =: m1
times. If A1 (q) = 0, we use the A = 0 quadratic distribution; if A1 (q) ∈ {1, 2}, we use the A ̸= 0 quadratic
distribution. This gives exactly Proposition 52(I.b).
Subcase (I.c): β ≥ 2. The admissibility condition is u3 ≡ −q (mod 3β ). Since (Z/3β Z)× is cyclic of
order 2 · 3β−1 , the cube map has image consisting precisely of the units congruent to ±1 modulo 9. Hence
there are no solutions if δ9 (q) = 0.
   Assume now δ9 (q) = 1. Then there are exactly three roots of u3 ≡ −q (mod 3β ). For such a root class
u, the forced residue A is given by (145). Moreover,
                              (u + 3β t)3 ≡ u3    (mod 3β+1 )        (t ∈ {0, 1, 2}),
so a root class modulo 3β either lifts to all three classes modulo 3β+1 or to none.         It lifts precisely when
A = 0, and because there are again exactly three roots modulo 3β+1 , exactly one of the three root classes
has A = 0, while the other two have A ̸= 0.
   Each root class modulo 3β has 3r−3β lifts to modulo 3r−2β , and by (146) each fixed pair (B, C) therefore
occurs
                            3r−3β L> = 3r−3β · 32v+r+4β−2 = 32r+2v+β−2 =: mβ
times for each root class. Hence the total type distribution is one copy of the A = 0 quadratic distribution
and two copies of the A ̸= 0 quadratic distribution. Therefore
                  N0 (v, r, β) = mβ ,     N(13 ) (v, r, β) = 2mβ ,     N(1,12 ) (v, r, β) = 12mβ ,
                              N(1,1,1) (v, r, β) = 6mβ ,     N(1,2) (v, r, β) = 6mβ ,
which is exactly Proposition 52(I.c).
Step 4: the boundary case r = 3β. Now the residue form is
                                  fA,B,C (x, y) = x3 − Ax2 y + Bxy 2 − Cy 3 .
The same argument as above shows that A is still forced by (145). Fix an admissible u and fix B, C ∈ F3 .
Using (144) with r = 3β, the condition
                                                 E
                                               C≡     (mod 3)
                                                33β
is equivalent to one linear congruence modulo 32β in α,
                                 −u2 α + 3β uη ≡ λ(u, A, B, C)       (mod 32β ),
118                                    TAIWANG DENG AND MALORS ESPINOSA

with u2 a unit. Therefore, for fixed admissible (u, B, C), the number of lifts is
(147)                                  L= := 3v+4β−1 · 3v+3β−1 = 32v+7β−2 .
So again, once u is fixed, the pairs (B, C) are equidistributed.
   We now need the corresponding cubic type counts.
   Let
                                         gA (T ) := T 3 − AT 2 + BT − C.
If A = 0, then g0 (T ) = T 3 + BT − C.
      • If B = 0, then g0 (T ) = T 3 − C = (T − c)3 for the unique c ∈ F3 with c3 = C, so all three choices
        of C give type (13 ).
      • If B = 1, then T 7→ T 3 + T is a permutation of F3 , so each C gives exactly one root, hence type
        (1, 2).
      • If B = 2, then g0 (T ) = T 3 − T − C. Since T 3 − T vanishes identically on F3 , the case C = 0 has
        the three distinct roots 0, 1, 2, hence type (1, 1, 1), while C = 1, 2 have no root, hence type (3).
So for fixed A = 0 the 9 pairs (B, C) contribute
                   1 of type (1, 1, 1),     3 of type (13 ),   3 of type (1, 2),   2 of type (3),
and none of type (1, 12 ).
   If A ̸= 0, the two possibilities A = 1 and A = 2 are equivalent up to the change of variable T 7→ −T , so
it suffices to treat A = 1. Then
                                g1 (T ) = T 3 − T 2 + BT − C,        g1′ (T ) = T + B.
For each B ∈ F3 , there is exactly one C for which g1 and g1′ have a common root; hence there are exactly
three repeated-root polynomials. A triple root is impossible because in characteristic 3 a cube (T − a)3
has no T 2 -term, whereas g1 has coefficient −1. Thus these three repeated-root cases are all of type (1, 12 ).
   A separable polynomial with three distinct roots in F3 would have roots 0, 1, 2, whose sum is 0, contra-
dicting the coefficient of T 2 , namely −1. Hence there is no type (1, 1, 1) when A ̸= 0.
   Finally, for each t ∈ F3 and each B ∈ F3 , there is a unique C such that g1 (t) = 0, so across all 9 pairs
(B, C) there are exactly 9 root incidences. The three type-(1, 12 ) polynomials contribute 6 incidences,
leaving 3 incidences. Since there is no completely split case, these 3 incidences must come from exactly
three polynomials of type (1, 2); the remaining three are therefore of type (3).
   So for fixed A ̸= 0 the 9 pairs (B, C) contribute
                             3 of type (1, 12 ),    3 of type (1, 2),      3 of type (3),
and none of the other two types.
Subcase (II.a): β = 1 (so r = 3). As before, admissibility is u ≡ −q (mod 3), so there is a unique class
of u, and the forced value of A is A1 (q). By (147), each fixed pair (B, C) occurs exactly
                                           L= = 32v+5 = 32r+2v−1 =: m1
times. If A1 (q) = 0, we use the A = 0 cubic distribution above; if A1 (q) ∈ {1, 2}, we use the A ̸= 0 cubic
distribution. This gives exactly Proposition 52(II.a).
Subcase (II.b): β ≥ 2. Again there are no admissible u if δ9 (q) = 0. Assume δ9 (q) = 1. Then there
are exactly three root classes modulo 3β , exactly one with A = 0 and two with A ̸= 0, by the same lifting
argument used in Subcase (I.c). Here u is taken modulo 3β , so there are no further lifts in u, and by (147)
each fixed pair (B, C) contributes
                                        L= = 32v+7β−2 = 32r+2v+β−2 =: mβ
                                                                                                                                         119

for each root class. Therefore the total type distribution is one copy of the A = 0 cubic distribution and
two copies of the A ̸= 0 cubic distribution, namely
                 N(1,1,1) (v, r, β) = mβ ,            N(13 ) (v, r, β) = 3mβ ,          N(1,12 ) (v, r, β) = 6mβ ,
                                    N(1,2) (v, r, β) = 9mβ ,             N(3) (v, r, β) = 8mβ .
This is exactly Proposition 52 (II.b).
  Combining all subcases proves Proposition 52.                                                                                          □

D.4. Main theorem.
Theorem 10. We have
                                 (x − 3)(x2 − 3)         (1 − 3−z−1 )(1 − 3−2z−1 )
             D3 (z) =                                  =                                                       (x = 3−z ).
